Answering the Objection on Miracles as Proof
Author refutes objection by analogy to divine signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that a miracle alone does not prove a prophet's truthfulness, by comparing it to God's method of establishing His own divinity through signs.

Core Idea
Miracles are divine definitions, like cosmic signs proving God's existence.

Some skeptics argue that a miracle occurring alongside a claim of prophethood is not sufficient proof—after all, miracles could theoretically accompany any claim. The author responds by drawing a powerful analogy: just as God proves His own divinity through unmistakable signs in creation, He proves the truthfulness of prophets through miracles. This lesson unpacks that analogy and shows how miracles function as divine definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice how the author structures his refutation: first stating the objection, then presenting his response.
Pay attention to the key terms: المعجزة, الدلالة, التعريف.
2
Identify the objection
The author quotes the skeptics' argument: that a miracle's conjunction with a claim does not prove truthfulness, because the miracle could be linked to any claim. Underline this part.
3
Understand the analogy
The author compares God's way of proving prophethood (through miracles) to His way of proving divinity (through signs). Both are forms of 'definition' (تعريف) by God. Ask yourself: Why is this comparison powerful?
The analogy shows that miracles are not arbitrary but are God's deliberate acts of definition.
4
Examine the examples
The author gives two examples: Adam teaching the names to angels and Muhammad challenging with the Qur'an. In both, the opponents were unable to match the miracle, proving the prophet's truthfulness. Note how the challenge (تحدي) is key.
The incapacity of the opponents is what makes the miracle a proof.
5
See the chain of truthfulness
The passage concludes that the truthfulness of earlier prophets is confirmed by later ones (since they corroborate each other) and vice versa. This creates a continuous chain of truthful statements.
This is important for the Islamic belief in all prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجواب عن شبهات المنكرين أن نقول : قولكم: إن اقتران المعجزة بدعوى المدعي لا ينتهض دليلا على صدقه، فإن نفس الاقتران بالإضافة إلى دعواه أو إلى غيرها من الأفعال أو الأقوال بمثابة واحدة). فنقول : سبيل تعريف الله تعالى عباده صدق الرسل بالآيات الخارقة للعادة كسبيل تعريفه إياهم إلهيته بالآيات الدالة عليها، والتعريف قد يكون بالقول تارة، وقد يكون بالفعل أخرى والتعريف بالقول قد يكون إخبارًا عن صدقه ؛ كقوله تعالى للملائكة : ( إِنِّي جَاعِلٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ خَلِيفَةً ) البقرة : 30، وقد يكون تنبيها على صدقه بتعجيز الخلق عن معارضته كما علم آدم الأسماء كلها ثم عرضهم على الملائكة، وكما علم المصطفى القرآن وقال : ( فَأْتُواْ بِسُورَةٍ مِّن مِثْلِهِ ) البقرة : 23] فكما عجزت الملائكة عن معارضة آدم بالأسماء عجزت العرب والعجم عن معارضة المصطفى بآيات القرآن ودلت الأسماء والآيات على صدق النبي الأول والنبي الآخر، ولما ثبت صدق الأول كان مبشرًا بمن بعده إلى الآخر، ولما ثبت صدق الآخر كان هو مصدقا لمن قبله إلى الأول، فكانت الأقوال متواصلة.
Then the answer to the doubts of the deniers is that we say: Your statement that the conjunction of the miracle with the claim of the claimant does not constitute a proof of his truthfulness—for the conjunction itself, whether related to his claim or to other actions or sayings, is equivalent—then we say: The way of God Almighty defining to His servants the truthfulness of messengers through miracles breaking customary habits is like His way of defining to them His divinity through the signs indicating it. And the definition may be by speech sometimes, and by action at other times. And the definition by speech may be an announcement of his truthfulness, like His saying to the angels: 'Indeed, I will make upon the earth a successive authority' (Al-Baqarah 2:30), and it may be a notification of his truthfulness by incapacitating creation from opposing him, as He taught Adam all the names, then presented them to the angels, and as He taught the Chosen One (Muhammad) the Qur'an and said: 'Then bring forth a surah like it' (Al-Baqarah 2:23). Just as the angels were unable to oppose Adam with the names, so the Arabs and non-Arabs were unable to oppose the Chosen One with the verses of the Qur'an, and the names and verses indicated the truthfulness of the first prophet and the last prophet. And when the truthfulness of the first was established, it was a herald for those after him up to the last, and when the truthfulness of the last was established, it was a confirmatory of those before him up to the first, so the statements were continuous.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against miracles as proofs of prophethood. The objectors claim that the mere conjunction of a miracle with a claim of prophethood does not logically imply truthfulness, because the miracle could be associated with other actions or statements equally. The author responds by comparing God's method of establishing prophetic truth through miracles to His method of establishing His own divinity through signs in the universe. Both are forms of definition (تعريف) by God. He explains that definition can be through speech (e.g., God telling the angels He will create a caliph) or through action (e.g., teaching Adam the names and then challenging the angels, or teaching Muhammad the Qur'an and challenging humanity to produce a surah like it). In both cases, the act of teaching and the subsequent challenge serve as a demonstration of truth by rendering opponents helpless. The angels could not produce names like Adam, and the Arabs could not produce a surah like the Qur'an. This incapacity proves the truthfulness of the prophet. Furthermore, the truth of earlier prophets is confirmed by later ones, and the truth of later ones confirms the earlier ones, creating a chain of truthful statements.

Key Terms
المعجزة
الدلالة
التعريف
الصدق
التحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle is a general sign that does not specifically prove the truth of a particular claim; it could accompany any statement.
Correction: The author argues that God uses miracles as a specific definition of prophetic truth, analogous to how He uses signs to define His own divinity. The miracle, especially when combined with a challenge, uniquely identifies the true prophet.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the purpose of a miracle that includes a challenge (like teaching Adam names or revealing the Qur'an)?
A) To show the power of the prophet.
B) To render the opponents helpless, thereby proving the prophet's truthfulness.
C) To replace the need for speech.
D) To establish a new religion.

Key Takeaway
Miracles are not mere coincidences but divine definitions that prove prophetic truth, just as cosmic signs prove God's divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If miracles are divine definitions directly from God to you, have you ever treated a clear sign—like the Quran's challenge or a personal answer to prayer—as a mere coincidence instead of a personal call from your Lord? What would it take for you to respond to every sign as if it were God speaking directly to you?
?
The objectors saw the conjunction of miracle and claim as neutral. In your own life, when you see God's guidance and His messengers' teachings, do you sometimes treat them as neutral options rather than decisive proofs? How can you cultivate a heart that recognizes divine definitions immediately?
?
This lesson shows that Adam's truth and Muhammad's truth are mutually reinforcing. Does your belief in one prophet strengthen your belief in all prophets, or do you have gaps? What practical step can you take this week to deepen your connection to the entire prophetic chain?

Distinguishing Miracles from Magic and Natural Properties
Al-Shahrastani distinguishes miracles from magic and natural properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between miracles and other extraordinary events according to al-Shahrastani: miracles are divine acts without any human intervention, unlike magic or natural wonders.

Core Idea
Miracles are God's direct acts, free from any human trickery or effort.

Al-Shahrastani anticipates an objection: critics claim miracles are just magic or natural properties. In this passage, he dismantles that objection by showing that genuine miracles lack any human involvement, unlike magical acts that rely on acquired tricks. This lays the foundation for the Islamic understanding of prophethood's divine confirmation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The passage begins by addressing those who reduce miracles to natural properties, astrology, or magic. Recognize this as a challenge to the unique status of prophetic miracles.
The objection is common in interfaith debates.
2
Identify the key contrast
Al-Shahrastani admits wonders can occur through natural properties and incantations, but he notes they always involve human effort: actions, timing, tools, etc. This is the first part of the distinction.
These are 'acquired tricks' (حيل كسبية).
3
Analyze the miracle example
He then gives the example of resurrecting dead bones—something impossible by human means. This act is done by God alone, without any preparation from the challenger. This is the second part: no human causality.
The example is deliberately extreme.
4
Grasp the theological principle
The passage concludes by referencing the earlier principle: an act upon a possible thing indicates the agent's will. When God performs such an act after a prophet's claim, it shows His intent to verify the prophet.
This connects miracles to divine confirmation of prophethood.

وأما الجواب عن ردهم الخوارق إلى الخواص والتنجيم وعلم السحر والطلسمات، قلنا: لا ينكر العاقل حصول العجائب من الخواص والعزائم، لكن أمثال هذه الغرائب ليست تخلو قط عن حيل كسبية تنضاف إليها من مباشرة فعل وجمع شيء إلى شيء، واختيار وقت، وتعزيم قول، وإعداد آلة، واستعداد مادة، وبالجملة من مزاولة فعل وقول من جهة المدعي؛ بحيث يتبين للناظر أن ذلك ليس فعلا لله تعالى أنشأه في الحال تصديقاً لدعوته، بخلاف المعجزة فإن كل عاقل يعلم بالضرورة أن عظاما رفانًا رمامًا تجتمع فتحيا شخصا وتتشخص حيا من غير معالجة من جهة المتحدي ولا مزاولة أمر عنه لا يكون ذلك إلا فعلا وصنعا من الله تعالى يظهر منه قصده إلى تصديق رسوله، فإنا قد ذكرنا أن الفعل ببعض الجائزات يدل على إرادة الفاعل، فإذا فعله عقيب دعوته بتخصيصه مقترنا بدعواه وهو صادق في نفسه دل على قصده إلى تصديقه وتخصيصه لرسالته.
As for the response to their rejection of miracles by attributing them to properties, astrology, magic, and talismans, we say: The intelligent person does not deny the occurrence of wonders from properties and incantations, but such marvels are never free from acquired tricks added to them, from direct action and combining one thing with another, choice of time, uttering incantations, preparation of tools, preparation of material, in short, from engagement in action and speech on the part of the claimant, such that it becomes clear to the observer that this is not an act of God Almighty that He creates instantaneously in verification of his claim. Unlike the miracle, for every intelligent person knows necessarily that decomposed bones that are decayed gather and become a living person, and become a living individual without any treatment from the challenger or any engagement of his, that is only the act and creation of God Almighty, which shows His intent to verify His messenger. For we have mentioned that the act in some possible things indicates the will of the agent. So if He does it after his call, with specification, accompanied by his claim, and he is truthful in himself, it indicates His intent to verify him and specify him for his message.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a response to the objection that miracles can be explained by natural properties, astrology, magic, etc. The author distinguishes between wonders that involve human effort (acquired tricks) and genuine miracles that occur without any human intervention, such as bringing dead bones to life. He argues that such an act can only be from God and shows His intent to support the prophet's truthfulness. This is a standard kalam argument for miracles as divine confirmation of prophethood.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that miracles are essentially the same as magic or natural wonders, just more impressive or performed by prophets.
Correction: Al-Shahrastani clarifies that genuine miracles are categorically different because they involve no human effort, preparation, or acquired tricks. They are direct divine acts that only God can perform, such as resurrecting the dead.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shahrastani, what distinguishes a miracle from other extraordinary events?
A) Miracles are performed by prophets only.
B) Miracles occur without any human effort or preparation.
C) Miracles always involve the resurrection of the dead.
D) Miracles are more spectacular than magic.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is a divine act that occurs without any human intervention, directly confirming a prophet's truthfulness, unlike other wonders that involve human effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a time you sought a 'miracle' but relied on human effort, formulas, or rituals. How does this passage challenge your understanding of what truly comes from God alone?
?
If miracles are free from human causation, does your faith rest on the direct intervention of God or on religious practices you perform? Where does your trust truly lie?

Distinguishing Miracles from Sorcery and Talismans
Answer to the claim that miracles can be reduced to sorcery and talismans

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to distinguish true prophetic miracles from other extraordinary events like sorcery and talismans based on the absence of human craft and the presence of divine specification in response to a prophetic challenge.

Core Idea
Miracles are God's direct act without human artifice, unlike sorcery which depends on acquired stratagems.

This passage addresses a common challenge: that prophetic miracles are no different from sorcery, talismans, or natural properties. The author concedes that such marvels occur, but draws a sharp line: true miracles are distinguished by the complete absence of any human manipulation or preparation. This lesson walks you through the argument, including the rational proof that the timing of a miracle in response to a claim of prophethood indicates divine affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its full English translation to get a sense of the overall argument. Note the structure: concession, distinction, proof, analogy, conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage contrasts wonders from properties/sorcery with true miracles. List the characteristics of each: sorcery involves acquired stratagems (ḥiyal kasbiyya) like action, time, incantation; miracles involve no such human involvement.
This is the key distinction.
3
Understand the logical proof
The author argues that an act specific to a time (the prophetic challenge) indicates a specific divine will. Just as specification in form/measure indicates will, specification in timing indicates God's intent to affirm the prophet.
Focus on the analogy with answered prayer.
4
Reflect on the answered prayer analogy
The passage gives an analogy: a person with an accepted supplication prays and is answered – they know God specified that answer for them. Similarly, the miracle specifies the prophet as truthful.
This helps solidify the reasoning.
5
Summarize the conclusion
The passage ends by emphasizing God's freedom in creating and choosing. The miracle is a divine act that cannot be reduced to human artifice.
This is the takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن ردهم الخوارق إلى الخواص والتنجيم وعلم السحر والطلسمات، قلنا: لا ينكر العاقل حصول العجائب من الخواص والعزائم، لكن أمثال هذه الغرائب ليست تخلو قط عن حيل كسبية تنضاف إليها من مباشرة فعل وجمع شيء إلى شيء، واختيار وقت، وتعزيم قول، وإعداد آلة، واستعداد مادة، وبالجملة من مزاولة فعل وقول من جهة المدعي؛ بحيث يتبين للناظر أن ذلك ليس فعلا الله تعالى أنشأه في الحال تصديقاً لدعوته، بخلاف المعجزة فإن كل عاقل يعلم بالضرورة أن عظاما رفانًا رمامًا تجتمع فتحيا شخصا وتتشخص حيا من غير معالجة من جهة المتحدي ولا مزاولة أمر عنه لا يكون ذلك إلا فعلا وصنعا من الله تعالى يظهر منه قصده إلى تصديق رسوله، فإنا قد ذكرنا أن الفعل ببعض الجائزات يدل على إرادة الفاعل، فإذا فعله عقيب دعوته بتخصيصه مقترنا بدعواه وهو صادق في نفسه دل على قصده إلى تصديقه وتخصيصه لرسالته ولو قدر ظهور مثل تلك المعجزة على يد كاذب لم يجز أن تقترن بدعواه النبوة، بل يصرف عنه داعيه الدعوى ضرورة حتى لا ينقلب الدليل شبهة ولا ينسحم الطريق على قدرة الله تعالى في إظهار التصديق بالآيات، وكما إذا اختص الفعل بوقت وقدر وشكل دل على إرادة مخصصة بذلك الوقت والقدر والشكل ولن يقدر وقوعه اتفاقا، كذلك إذا اختص الفعل بوقت تحدي المتحدي دل ذلك على أنه إنما أراد تخصيصه بالصدق، وكما لم يختلف وجه الدليل العقلي على أصل الإرادة بأصل التخصيص لم يختلف وجه الدليل على وجه تعلق الإرادة بتخصيص التصديق، فافهم هذه الدقيقة هذا كمن علم أن له عند الله دعوة مستجابة فدعا واستجيب له علم ضرورة أن المجيب أراد تخصيصه بتلك الإجابة كرامة له وإنعاما عليه، ولم يقدح في علمه ذلك تجويز المجوز أن ذلك ربما يقع اتفاقا أو بسبب آخر، فإذا كان مثل ذلك معلوماً في الجزيات لكل شخص شخص عرف مواقع نعم الله تعالى على عبده، وكان بصيراً بزمانه مقبلاً على شأنه، فما ظنك بحال من اصطفاه الله تعالى من خليقته واجتباه من بريته، وقد تبين أنه يخلق ما يشاء ويختار ما كان لهم الخيرة من أمرهم.
As for the answer to their dismissal of miracles (kharq al-ʿādah) as due to properties (khawāṣṣ), astrology, sorcery (siḥr), and talismans (ṭilasmāt), we say: The rational person does not deny the occurrence of marvels from properties and incantations, but such wonders are never devoid of acquired stratagems (ḥiyal kasbiyya) that are added to them from direct action, gathering one thing to another, choosing a time, reciting an incantation, preparing an instrument, and preparing matter; in short, from engaging in action and speech on the part of the claimant, so that the observer perceives that it is not an act of God which He created spontaneously in affirmation of his claim. Unlike the miracle (muʿjiza), for every rational person knows necessarily that decayed, crumbling bones that come together and become a living person and become distinctly alive without any manipulation from the challenger or any activity on his part—that is nothing but an act and creation of God Almighty, from which His intention to affirm His messenger appears. For we have mentioned that an act performed among possible things indicates the will of the agent. So if He performs it after his prophetic claim, specifically, in conjunction with his claim, while he is truthful in himself, it indicates His intention to affirm him and specify him for His message. And if it were supposed that such a miracle appeared at the hand of a liar, it would not be permissible for it to be connected to his claim of prophethood; rather, the incentive for the claim would necessarily divert it from him, so that the proof does not turn into a doubt and the path is not blocked due to God's power in showing affirmation through signs. Just as when an act is specific to a time, measure, and form, it indicates a will that specifies that time, measure, and form, and it cannot happen by chance, similarly, when the act is specific to the time of the challenger's challenge, it indicates that He only intended to specify him for truth. And just as the manner of the rational proof for the principle of will is not different due to the principle of specification, so the manner of the proof does not differ regarding the manner of the will's connection to specifying affirmation. Understand this subtle point. This is like someone who knows that he has a supplication accepted by God, so he supplicates and it is answered; he knows necessarily that the Responder intended to specify him with that answer as an honor and favor to him, and his knowledge is not undermined by the possibility of someone supposing that it might happen by chance or due to another cause. So if such a thing is known in particulars to each individual, and he recognizes the places of God's blessings on His servant, and he is discerning of his time and attentive to his affair, what do you think of the state of one whom God has chosen from His creation and selected from His creatures? And it has become clear that He creates what He wills and chooses; they have no choice in their affair.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the objection that miracles (khawāriq) can be reduced to natural properties (khawāṣṣ), astrology, sorcery (siḥr), and talismans (ṭilasmāt). The author, likely al-Shahrastānī, begins by conceding that rational people do not deny that marvels can occur through properties and incantations. However, he immediately distinguishes them from miracles: such wonders never lack some acquired stratagem (ḥīla kasbiyya) involving an act, gathering materials, choosing a time, reciting an incantation, preparing an instrument, or readying matter. The observer can see that these are not a direct act of God created instantaneously to affirm the claimant's message.

In contrast, a true miracle (muʿjiza) is something that every rational person knows necessarily: that decayed, crumbling bones can come together and become a living person without any manipulation or activity from the challenger. Such an event can only be an act and creation (ṣunʿ) of God, showing His intention to affirm His messenger. The author recalls an earlier principle: an act among possible things indicates the will of the agent. So when God performs that act after the prophetic claim, specifically in connection with the claim while the claimant is truthful, it demonstrates God's intention to affirm him and specify him for His message.

The author then addresses a potential objection: could a miracle occur at the hand of a liar? He answers that it would not be connected to the liar's claim of prophethood; rather, the incentive for the claim would be diverted from him, preserving the proof from becoming a doubt and not obstructing God's power to show affirmation through signs.

To illustrate the logical connection, he draws an analogy: just as an act specific to a time, measure, and form indicates a will that specifies those particulars and cannot occur by chance, so an act specific to the time of the challenger's challenge indicates that God intended to specify him for truth. The rational proof for the principle of will and its specification does not differ.

The author then gives a further analogy: a person who knows he has an accepted supplication and prays and is answered knows necessarily that God intended to specify him with that answer as an honor; his certainty is not undermined by the possibility that it could happen by chance or due to another cause. If such knowledge is possible in ordinary cases, how much more so for one whom God has chosen from creation. The passage concludes by emphasizing God's absolute freedom in creating and choosing, with no choice for others.

muʿjiza
kharq al-ʿādah
khawāṣṣ
siḥr
ṭilasmāt
ʿazāʾim
ḥiyal kasbiyya

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every extraordinary event qualifies as a miracle.
Correction: True miracles are distinguished by the absence of any human craft or preparation; they are direct acts of God.

Checkpoint

B) A miracle occurs without any human manipulation or preparation, while other marvels involve acquired stratagems.
C) A miracle can be performed by both prophets and non-prophets.
D) A miracle is always accompanied by a prophesy.

Key Takeaway
A true miracle is a direct, unmediated act of God that occurs in response to a prophetic challenge, serving as a definitive sign of prophethood, unlike sorcery which depends on human craft.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness something extraordinary in your life, do you immediately attribute it to natural causes or to God's direct intervention? Are you like the rational person who denies miracles? What would it take for you to see God's hand directly, without human mediation?
?
Have you ever relied on 'acquired stratagems' (like meticulous planning, networking, or skills) to the point of forgetting that true change comes only from God? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in His direct action, especially in areas where you feel most capable?
?
The passage says one who knows his supplication is answered knows necessarily that God intended to honor him. Do you live with that certainty? Or do you doubt God's personal attention to you? What specific supplication could you make today that would test your certainty of His response, and how would you receive the answer?

The Impossibility of a Miracle on a Liar: The Argument from Specification
Proof that a miracle cannot occur on a liar due to specific timing and analogy of answered prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a genuine miracle cannot be performed by a false prophet, based on the rational argument from specification (takhṣīṣ).

Core Idea
Specific timing of a miracle proves divine confirmation, not chance.

In this passage, the author presents a rational proof that miracles must accompany truthful prophecy. He uses the concept of specification (takhṣīṣ) to show that when a miracle occurs at the precise moment of a challenge, it indicates God's will to confirm the claimant's truth. He then draws an analogy to answered prayer to reinforce the point. This argument is central to distinguishing genuine miracles from mere coincidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the translation. Notice how the argument is structured around the idea of specification.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Understand specification (takhṣīṣ) and will (irāda)
The author argues that the specific timing, measure, and form of an action indicate a deliberate will. This is the key premise.
Specification is the sign of will, not chance.
3
Follow the analogy to answered prayer
The answered prayer example shows that specificity proves divine intention, and this knowledge is not undermined by possibilities of chance.
The analogy reinforces the same logic for miracles.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does the author say the proof would become a doubt if a liar could perform a miracle? Consider the role of miracles as evidence.
Miracles must be clear signs, not ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قدر ظهور مثل تلك المعجزة على يد كاذب لم يجز أن تقترن بدعواه النبوة، بل يصرف عنه داعيه الدعوى ضرورة حتى لا ينقلب الدليل شبهة ولا ينسحم الطريق على قدرة الله تعالى في إظهار التصديق بالآيات، وكما إذا اختص الفعل بوقت وقدر وشكل دل على إرادة مخصصة بذلك الوقت والقدر والشكل ولن يقدر وقوعه اتفاقا، كذلك إذا اختص الفعل بوقت تحدي المتحدي دل ذلك على أنه إنما أراد تخصيصه بالصدق، وكما لم يختلف وجه الدليل العقلي على أصل الإرادة بأصل التخصيص لم يختلف وجه الدليل على وجه تعلق الإرادة بتخصيص التصديق، فافهم هذه الدقيقة هذا كمن علم أن له عند الله دعوة مستجابة فدعا واستجيب له علم ضرورة أن المجيب أراد تخصيصه بتلك الإجابة كرامة له وإنعاما عليه، ولم يقدح في علمه ذلك تجويز المجوز أن ذلك ربما يقع اتفاقا أو بسبب آخر، فإذا كان مثل ذلك معلوماً في الجزيات لكل شخص شخص عرف مواقع نعم الله تعالى على عبده ، وكان بصيراً بزمانه مقبلاً على شأنه، فما ظنك بحال من اصطفاه الله تعالى من خليقته واجتباه من بريته، وقد تبين أنه يخلق ما يشاء ويختار ما كان لهم الخيرة من أمرهم
And if it were possible for the appearance of such a miracle at the hand of a liar, it would not be permissible for it to be associated with his claim of prophethood; rather, the caller would divert him from the claim out of necessity, so that the proof does not become a doubt and the path is not hidden concerning the power of Allah, exalted, in manifesting confirmation by signs. And just as when an action is specified by a time, a measure, and a form, it indicates a will specifying that time, measure, and form, and it cannot occur by coincidence, similarly, when an action is specified by the time of the challenger's challenge, that indicates that He only intended to specify it with truth. And just as the aspect of the rational proof for the principle of will through the principle of specification is not different, the aspect of the proof for the mode of the will's attachment to specifying confirmation is not different. So understand this subtle point. This is like one who knows that he has an answered supplication with Allah, so he supplicates and it is answered; he knows necessarily that the Responder intended to specify him with that answer as an honor and a favor to him. And his knowledge is not invalidated by the possibility that it might occur by coincidence or due to some other cause. So if such a thing is known in particulars to every individual person, and he knows the places of Allah's blessings upon His servant, and is insightful about his time and attentive to his affair, then what do you think of the state of one whom Allah has chosen from His creation and selected from His creatures? And it has become clear that He creates what He wills and chooses, and they have no choice in their affair.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is a proof that a genuine miracle cannot be performed by a false claimant to prophethood. The author argues from two analogies: first, the specific timing, measure, and form of an action indicate a deliberate will rather than chance; second, the case of an answered prayer whose specificity proves divine intention. He concludes that the same reasoning applies to miracles. The passage uses 'specification' (takhṣīṣ) as indicating will (irāda). The argument is that if a miracle is performed at the moment of challenge (taḥaddī), it shows God intends to confirm the truth of the claimant. The author warns not to be confused by those who might attribute it to chance or other causes, just as knowledge from an answered prayer is not undermined by such possibilities.

Correction: The author argues that the specific timing and context of a miracle eliminate coincidence; a miracle at the time of challenge is a clear sign of divine confirmation, not a test or chance.

?

Have you ever felt a clear sign pointing you towards obedience—like a verse that struck you or an event that could only be divine—yet you dismissed it as chance? What is one sign you have been avoiding, and what will you do about it today?

What is one specific change you can make today to align your schedule with God's specification for your life?

First Objection: Repeated Miracles Become Ordinary
Objection that repeated miracles become ordinary and could be confused with end-time phenomena.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the epistemological challenge posed by the objection that repeated miracles become ordinary and could be confused with end-time phenomena.

Core Idea
Miracles lose their proof if they become ordinary or confused with eschatology.

This passage presents the first objection from deniers against the argument from miracles. They argue that if miracles happen repeatedly, they become ordinary and thus lose their evidentiary value. Furthermore, extraordinary events at the end of time could be mistaken for prophetic miracles. Sanusi must respond to this challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the deniers present their objection with two distinct concerns.
Focus on the logical flow from repetition to ordinariness, and then to eschatological confusion.
2
Identify the two prongs of the objection
Separate the objection into its two main parts: (1) repeated miracles become ordinary, (2) end-time events could be confused with prophetic miracles. Understand how each undermines the proof value of a miracle.
The deniers use the example of eschatological signs to illustrate their point.
3
Consider the implication for miracle proofs
Think about why this objection is serious: if accepted, it would mean that no single miracle can provide certain knowledge of a prophet's truthfulness. Reflect on how a defender might respond.
This sets the stage for Sanusi's response in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المنكرون ولنا أسئلة أخرى وجب عليكم الجواب عنها. منها أن الخارق للعادة إذا تكرر وتوالى صار معتادًا بالاتفاق، فما يؤمنكم في الحالة الأولى أنه من المتكررات المعتادة في ثاني الحال، وإن لم يوجد في سابق الأحوال، ألستم تشترطون أن يكون الخارق في زمن التكليف، فإن في آخر الزمان تنخرق العادات كلها، فتتكور الشمس، وتتكدر النجوم، وتسير الجبال، وتنشق السماء، وتزلزل الأرض؟ فلو ادعى مدع أن هذه وأمثالها معجزاتي، وهي بالاتفاق خوارق لا تندرج تحت قوة البشر، ولا يتوصل إليه بالحيل بل تمحضت فعلا الله تعالى، فلا يكون ذلك دليلا على دعوى هذا المدعي، فالعاقل في زمن التكليف ربما يتوقع أن تتوالى الآيات فلا يحصل له العلم بكونها آية على صدقه ما لم يأمن التوالي والتعاقب، بل يورثه ذلك توقفا وتربصا.
The deniers said: 'We have other questions that you must answer. Among them is that a miracle (kharq al-'āda), if repeated and successive, becomes ordinary by agreement. So what assures you in the first case that it is among the ordinary things that will become common in the second state, even if it did not exist in previous states? Do you not require that the miracle occurs in the time of obligation? For at the end of time all habits will be broken: the sun will be wrapped up, the stars will be obscured, the mountains will be set in motion, the sky will be split, and the earth will be shaken. So if a claimant says: "These and their like are my miracles," and they are by agreement extraordinary, not within human power, and not attainable by tricks, but are purely the act of Allah, then that would not be a proof for the claim of this claimant. For a rational person, in the time of obligation, might expect the signs to follow in succession, so he would not obtain certain knowledge that it is a sign of his truthfulness unless he is safe from succession and sequence; rather, that makes him hesitate and wait.'
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is the first objection raised by deniers against the argument from miracles. The objection argues that if miracles were to happen repeatedly, they would become ordinary and thus not serve as proofs. Moreover, in the end times, all laws of nature are broken, so extraordinary events could be attributed to eschatological phenomena rather than to a prophet's claim. The deniers challenge that the same conditions could apply to the first miracle, causing doubt. They claim that a rational person would not be convinced because he might expect more miracles to follow, making his knowledge uncertain.

Key Terms
kharq al-'āda
mu'jiz
zaman al-taklīf
ākhir al-zamān

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies that miracles can ever be proofs.
Correction: The objection does not deny the possibility of miracles; it challenges the certainty of their evidentiary value under certain conditions.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection's main point about repeated miracles?
A) They become ordinary
B) They prove the claimant is a prophet
C) They are impossible
D) They are always accompanied by signs

Key Takeaway
The objection forces defenders of miracles to address the conditions under which a miracle remains a convincing proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness an extraordinary event that seems like a sign from Allah, how do you know whether it confirms a truth or is just a test or a coincidence? What criterion do you rely on that is not arbitrary? This objection challenges the certainty of prophetic signs—what makes you certain of the signs you follow in your own faith?

Think about a time you experienced something amazing—a prayer answered, a protection, a miracle. Did you eventually get used to it and lose the awe? The deniers say repeated miracles become ordinary; has any sign in your life become ordinary, and what does that reveal about your heart?

If you saw the sun being wrapped up and stars falling, would you immediately assume it is a prophet's miracle or the Day of Judgment? How prepared are you to distinguish a true messenger from the general confusion of signs? This lesson shows that even extraordinary events can be ambiguous—what effort have you made to learn the true criteria for a prophetic sign, and are you living in ready expectation or in complacency?

Second Objection to Miracle Proof
Objection that similar miracles could have occurred elsewhere or by others, so they don't prove the claimant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the proof of prophethood through miracles: that the possibility of similar miracles elsewhere or by others undermines the link between the miracle and the claimant's truthfulness.

Core Idea
Even a real miracle can fail to prove who performed it if rivals could have done the same.

The proof of prophethood via miracles relies on the uniqueness of the miracle and the audience's inability to oppose it. But what if a similar miracle had occurred in the past or in another land, or was performed by a different agent? This objection challenges the very logic that connects the miracle to the claimant, arguing that inability to oppose does not guarantee truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the objection draws a parallel between present and past/other places.
2
Identify the key premises
The objection states that rational people consider it possible for similar miracles to occur in the past, elsewhere, or by another. Understand each scenario.
3
Analyse the conclusion
The conclusion is that the audience's inability to oppose does not prove the claimant's truth. Why? Because others might not be unable.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is powerful. It does not deny the miracle's occurrence but questions its evidential value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن العقلاء كما يجوزون وقوع أمثاله في ثاني الحال كذلك يجوزون وقوع أمثاله في سالف الأيام، أو في قطر آخر من الأقطار، أو ظهور ذلك بفعل فاعل آخر، وإن كانوا عاجزين عن معارضته، فربما لا يعجز غيرهم، فعجزهم لا يدل على صدقه.
And among them is that the rational people, just as they deem it possible for the like of it (the miracle) to occur in the second state, similarly they deem it possible for the like of it to occur in past days, or in another region of the regions, or that it appears by the act of another agent, even if they are unable to oppose it. So perhaps others are not unable, so their inability does not indicate his truthfulness.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This is the second objection. The deniers argue that even if a miracle occurs, it could have parallels in history or in other places, or could have been performed by a different agent. The fact that the present audience cannot oppose it does not prove that others elsewhere cannot; therefore, the miracle does not conclusively prove the claimant's truth. This objection attacks the uniqueness of the miracle as a proof.

Key Terms
mu'jiz
aqlā'
mu'āraḍa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the objection requires the miracle to be unprecedented and that if it is unprecedented, the proof stands.
Correction: The objection does not deny the miracle's novelty; it argues that even if the miracle is novel, the possibility of similar acts elsewhere or by others breaks the necessary link between the miracle and the claimant.

Checkpoint

A) They are impossible
B) They are impossible to oppose
C) They could have occurred elsewhere or by others
D) They are always unique

Key Takeaway
The second objection argues that even a genuine miracle does not reliably identify its performer, because similar feats could have occurred elsewhere or by other agents.

The passage reminds us that inability to refute does not equal truth. Before this day ends, identify one such belief and actively seek out the strongest argument against it. Are you willing to let your belief be tested?

The objection argues that a miracle's location or timing could be replicated by someone else. In your own life, where have you assumed that your unique circumstances make your experiences unrepeatable? Have you ever used your particular situation as a shield against accountability, thinking that no one else could understand or judge? Repent from that isolation and place yourself under the same standards as others.

Third Objection: The Need for Individual Verification and Impracticality of Personal Miracles
Objection that doubt remains for everyone, requiring individual miracles for each person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the necessity of miracles: that if doubt must be removed for everyone, each person would require an individual miracle, leading to an impractical demand.

Core Idea
If everyone is in doubt, everyone needs a personal miracle — an impossible requirement.

In this passage, the deniers raise a clever objection: if the prophet's truthfulness must be verified for each person individually, then each person would need their own miracle. This challenges the proponents' insistence on certainty without providing a practical means for each individual to attain it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow from doubt to the need for individual miracles.
The passage ends abruptly; the objection is incomplete but the core logic is clear.
2
Identify the key claim
The objection states that doubt about the prophet's truthfulness exists for every person. Therefore, the removal of doubt must also apply to every person individually.
3
Understand the conclusion
This would require that each person or group be given a specific miracle. The objection points out that this is not required by the proponents' own principles.
The objection highlights an inconsistency in the proponents' stance.
4
Reflect on the objection
Consider why this objection is a problem for those who argue that miracles are necessary for faith. How might a proponent respond?
This step prepares you for the next lesson on possible responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الشك في صدقه لكل واحد من الناس فيجب أن يكون الرافع لكل واحد واحد وعلى مقتضى ذلك يلزم أن يخصص كل واحد أو كل جمع غيب أو حضور بمعجزة خاصة، وليس ذلك شرطًا على أصلكم، بل عندكم للشك

Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This is the third objection, though it appears incomplete. The deniers argue that if everyone is in doubt about the prophet's truthfulness, then each person would need a divine sign to remove their doubt. That would require a miracle for every individual or every group, which is impractical and not required by the proponents' own principles. The objection points to a logical inconsistency: if faith requires certainty, then everyone should have a direct miracle, but the proponents do not require that.

Key Terms

Correction: The objection is not demanding that everyone get a miracle; rather, it is pointing out that if doubt must be removed individually, then the proponents' own system would require individual miracles, which they do not accept. This reveals an inconsistency in their argument.

First Objection: Why Miracles Alone May Not Prove Prophethood
First objection: miracles may recur at end time, preventing certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against using miracles as definitive proof of prophethood, specifically the problem of potential recurrence and the lack of certainty it creates.

Core Idea
A miracle only proves truth if it is unique and not part of a chain of events.

This lesson presents the first objection raised against the evidential value of miracles. The opponent argues that if extraordinary events can occur repeatedly, as at the end of time, a rational person cannot be certain that any single event is a sign specifically confirming a prophet's claim. Let's examine this challenge closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is constructed as a rhetorical question.
Pay attention to the list of end-time events.
2
Identify the key conditions
The opponent refers to miracles occurring in the 'time of obligation' (زمن التكليف). He then argues that at the end of time, many extraordinary events happen, so a claimant could use them. Identify why these events would not serve as proof.
3
Reflect on the consequence
The passage concludes that the rational person would hesitate (توقف) and wait (تربص) because of the possibility of succession (توالي). Consider how this undermines certainty.
This connects to why prophets typically perform miracles in controlled settings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألستم تشترطون أن يكون الخارق في زمن التكليف، فإن في آخر الزمان تنخرق العادات كلها، فتتكور الشمس، وتتكدر النجوم، وتسير الجبال، وتنشق السماء، وتزلزل الأرض؟ فلو ادعى مدع أن هذه وأمثالها معجزاتي، وهي بالاتفاق خوارق لا تندرج تحت قوة البشر، ولا يتوصل إليه بالحيل بل تمحضت فعلا الله تعالى، فلا يكون ذلك دليلا على دعوى هذا المدعي، فالعاقل في زمن التكليف ربما يتوقع أن تتوالى الآيات فلا يحصل له العلم بكونها آية على صدقه ما لم يامن التوالي والتعاقب، بل يورثه ذلك توقفا وتربصا.
Do you not stipulate that the miracle occurs in the time of obligation? For at the end of time all customs will be broken: the sun will be wound up, the stars will become dark, the mountains will move, the sky will split, and the earth will quake. So if someone claims that these and their like are his miracles, and they are unanimously marvels that fall under no human power and cannot be attained by tricks, but are purely an act of Allah, then that would not be evidence for this claimant's claim. The rational person during the time of obligation perhaps expects that signs will follow one another, so he does not attain knowledge that it is a sign of his truthfulness as long as he does not feel secure from succession and sequence; rather, that causes him hesitation and waiting.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to using miracles as proof of prophethood. The opponent asks: Do you not require that the miracle occur during the time of obligation (i.e., the time when humans are accountable)? Because at the end of time, all natural customs will be broken: the sun will be coiled up, stars dim, mountains move, sky split, earth quake. If someone then claims that these events are his miracles, even though they are clearly beyond human power and purely divine acts, they would not serve as evidence for his claim. Why? Because a rational person during the time of obligation might expect that such extraordinary events will recur and follow one another, so he cannot be certain that a given event is a sign specifically confirming the claimant's truthfulness. The possibility of succession and repetition prevents him from attaining certainty; instead, it leaves him in hesitation and waiting. Thus, miracles cannot provide definitive proof if they are not unique and exclusive.

Key Terms

زمن التكليف
عادات
خوارق
آية
توالي
توقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary event automatically proves a prophet's truthfulness, regardless of context or uniqueness.
Correction: For a miracle to be probative, it must be uniquely tied to the claimant and not part of a series of expected or unconnected extraordinary events, as the end-time scenario shows.

Checkpoint

D) Because the person is not present to witness it.

Key Takeaway
This objection shows that for a miracle to be probative, it must be uniquely tied to the claimant and not part of a series of extraordinary events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The rational person hesitates when miracles could be part of a pattern. In your own spiritual life, where do you rush to claim certainty about Allah's will? Name one specific situation where you will now deliberately withhold judgment and wait for clearer evidence.

Second Objection: Miracles Could Be from Other Causes
Second objection: rational people allow similar miracles from other agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to miracles as proofs: that rational people allow similar miracles from other agents or times, so the inability of immediate observers to replicate does not prove the claimant's truthfulness.

Core Idea
A miracle's inimitability does not rule out other agents or times.

The passage presents the second objection to using miracles as proof of prophethood. It argues that rational people, while accepting a miracle in the present, logically must also accept the possibility of similar wonders in the past, other places, or by other agents. Therefore, the inability of the present audience to oppose the miracle is not sufficient to prove that the claimant is truthful.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: عقلاء (rational people), ثاني الحال (second state, i.e., present time), etc.
The objection is about logical consistency.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The objection starts with a premise about what rational people allow, then draws a conclusion about the inability of the audience. Identify each part.
3
Consider the implications
Think about why this objection is a problem for using miracles as proof. What additional condition would be needed to restore the probative force?
This connects to the broader discussion of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن العقلاء كما يجوزون وقوع أمثاله في ثاني الحال كذلك يجوزون وقوع أمثاله في سالف الأيام، أو في قطر آخر من الأقطار، أو ظهور ذلك بفعل فاعل آخر، وإن كانوا عاجزين عن معارضته، فربما لا يعجز غيرهم، فعجزهم لا يدل على صدقه.
Among them is that rational people, just as they allow the occurrence of its like in the second state, also allow the occurrence of its like in past days, or in another region of the earth, or its appearance by the act of another agent, even if they are unable to oppose it, perhaps others are not unable, so their inability does not indicate his truthfulness.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection. It argues that rational people, while allowing that a miracle could occur in the present, also allow that similar extraordinary events could have occurred in the past, or in another region, or could be produced by another agent. Even if the current audience is unable to produce a similar miracle, that does not prove the claimant's truthfulness, because others (perhaps in different times or places) might be able to do so. Therefore, the inability of the immediate observers to replicate the miracle does not establish that the miracle is exclusive to the claimant. The probative force of a miracle depends on its being beyond the power of all creatures, not just those present.

Key Terms
عقلاء
ثاني الحال
خارق
فاعل
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a miracle's inimitability by the immediate audience suffices to prove a prophet's truthfulness, ignoring the possibility of other agents.
Correction: The objection shows that the inability of the audience does not rule out the possibility that other agents (in other times/places) could produce a similar wonder, so the miracle is not uniquely tied to the claimant.

Checkpoint

A) Rational people deny that miracles occur.
B) Rational people allow that similar miracles could occur in other times or by other agents.
C) Rational people require that miracles be performed by the claimant only.
D) Rational people accept that inability to oppose implies truth.

Key Takeaway
The probative force of a miracle requires it to be beyond the power of all creatures, not just those present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objection shows that our inability to explain something does not automatically prove divine intervention. Are there areas in your life where you attribute events to Allah without verifying if they are truly from Him? How can you ensure your faith is based on sound evidence, not mere assumption?

Miracles

Third Objection: Need for Individual Miracles
Third objection: universal doubt requires individual miracles for each person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the sufficiency of a single miracle as proof of prophethood, focusing on the logical requirement of individual doubt-removal.

Core Idea
If everyone doubts, everyone needs their own miracle.

Sanusi presents a powerful logical objection: if doubt about a prophet's truthfulness is universal, then the removal of that doubt must be individual. This would require that each person—or at least each group—receive a special miracle. The objection challenges the standard view that a single miracle witnessed by experts suffices for all.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the objection begins with a logical premise, then contrasts it with the opponent's position, and ends with problematic consequences.
Pay attention to the key terms: شك, رافع, معجزة خاصة, تقليد, اعتياد.
2
Identify the logical requirement
Find the sentence that states: if doubt is universal, the removal must be individual. This is the core logical claim of the objection.
The Arabic: 'الشك في صدقه لكل واحد من الناس فيجب أن يكون الرافع لكل واحد واحد'.
3
Contrast with the opponent's view
The objection then states the opponent's actual position: a single miracle suffices for experts, and others follow by imitation (taqlid). Note that miracles are not required to continue indefinitely.
The opponent's view is introduced with 'بل عندكم'.
4
Reflect on the problem
The objection ends with two rhetorical questions: why are we obligated to believe past prophets (without seeing their miracles) and why were distant people in the prophet's own time obligated (without witnessing)? This shows the inconsistency.
The phrase 'وهذا مشكل' sums up the objection as a genuine problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الشك في صدقه لكل واحد من الناس فيجب أن يكون الرافع لكل واحد واحد وعلى مقتضى ذلك يلزم أن يخصص كل واحد أو كل جمع غيب أو حضور بمعجزة خاصة، وليس ذلك شرطًا على أصلكم، بل عندكم المعجزة الواحدة لجماعة من أهل الخبرة والبصيرة كافية، ويلزم التصديق على غيرهم من أهل التقليد ولا يجب استمرار المعجزة في كل زمن، بل لو استمرت خرجت عن الإعجاز والتحقت بالمعتاد، فماذا يلزمنا تصديق الأنبياء الماضين، ولم نجد في زماننا ما يدل على صدقهم؟ وبم يلزم أهل الأقطار في زمانه ولم يشاهدوا ما ظهر على يده من الآيات؟ وهذا مشكل.
Among them is that doubt about his truthfulness exists for every person, so the removal must be for each individual. And accordingly, it would be necessary to specify each person or each group, absent or present, with a special miracle. That is not a condition according to your principle; rather, with you, a single miracle is sufficient for a group of people of expertise and insight, and belief is incumbent upon others who are people of imitation, and it is not necessary for the miracle to continue in every time; rather, if it continued, it would leave the realm of miracle and join the realm of the customary. So why are we obliged to believe the past prophets when we do not find in our time anything indicating their truth? And why are the people of other regions in his time obliged when they did not witness what appeared at his hand of signs? This is problematic.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. It argues that if everyone is in doubt about the prophet's truthfulness, then the removal of that doubt should occur for each individual. This would require that each person or each group (whether present or absent) receive a special miracle tailored to them. But this is not your principle; rather, in your view, a single miracle for a group of expert eyewitnesses is sufficient, and others must follow by imitation. Furthermore, you do not require the miracle to continue throughout time—if it continued, it would become customary and lose its miraculous nature. So the objection asks: Why are we required to believe past prophets when we do not see their miracles today? And why were people in other regions (who did not witness the prophet's miracles) required to believe? This is a genuine problem. The objection challenges the consistency of the miraculous proof across time and space.

Key Terms
شك
رافع
معجزة خاصة
أهل الخبرة والبصيرة
تقليد
اعتياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a single miracle observed by a few suffices as proof for all people everywhere and at all times.
Correction: The objection argues that if doubt is universal, each person would need a special miracle; a single miracle for a group does not logically remove doubt for everyone, especially those not witnessing it.

Checkpoint
According to the third objection, what does universal doubt logically require?
A) That everyone accept the miracle on authority.
B) That each person receive a special miracle.
C) That miracles must continue forever.
D) That only eyewitnesses need to believe.

Key Takeaway
The third objection argues that universal doubt would logically require individual miracles for each person, making a single miracle insufficient as universal proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had been in a distant region during the Prophet's time and never saw his miracles, would you have believed? What basis would your faith have? Does that thought unsettle you?
?
When you call yourself a believer, are you relying on imitation (taqlid) of those who witnessed miracles, or have you personally confronted the evidence? How can you move from imitation to certainty?
?
This passage challenges the idea that a single miracle suffices for everyone. Does your own faith rest on a personal encounter with proof, or are you comfortable with second-hand belief? If the latter, why does that not trouble you?

Fourth Objection: God's Misguidance and Miracles for Liars
Fourth objection against tying miracles to prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the necessity of miracles as proof of prophethood, based on God's absolute power to misguide.

Core Idea
If God can misguide, miracles are not a reliable test of truthfulness.

This lesson presents a powerful objection: if God can misguide people, then He could also support a liar with miracles. This challenges the common assumption that miracles always indicate truthfulness. The passage argues that since God is not constrained, miracles do not necessarily accompany true prophets, nor are they withheld from false ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the rhetorical questions and examples used to build the objection.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if you allow misguidance, then what makes you feel...'
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument: premise (God can misguide) → consequence (miracles can appear at a liar's hand) → examples (prophets without miracles) → conclusion (your tying of truthfulness to miracles is unjustified).
The objection targets those who accept God's power to misguide but still insist miracles prove truthfulness.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this objection is important: if accepted, it undermines the common argument that miracles are necessary for certifying prophethood. Think about how a theologian might respond.
This objection challenges the very foundation of the miracle argument for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنكم إذا جوزتم الإضلال على الله تعالى فما يشعركم أنه يظهر الآيات على يدي كاذب ولا يظهرها على وفق صادق، فلا ذلك يضره في الإلهية، ولا هذا ينفعه في الربوبية؟ وكثير من الأنبياء قد خلت دعوتهم من المعجزة فمضوا على أمر الله دعاة للخلق من غير التفات إلى طلب الآيات منهم، ومن غير إكباب على طلب الآيات من الله تعالى فما بالكم ربطتم صدق الرسول بالمعجزات هذا الربط؟
Among them is that if you allow misguidance on the part of Allah, what makes you feel that He causes miracles to appear at the hand of a liar and does not cause them to appear in accordance with a truthful person? So that does not harm him in divinity, nor does this benefit him in lordship? Many prophets have had their call devoid of miracles, so they went on the command of God as callers to creation without paying attention to requesting signs from them, and without insisting on requesting signs from Allah. So why have you tied the truthfulness of the messenger to miracles in this way?
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection. It targets those who accept that God can misguide people (i.e., lead them astray). If God can misguide, then He could also cause miracles to appear at the hand of a liar without any detriment to His divinity, and He could withhold miracles from a truthful prophet without affecting His lordship. Since neither action is impossible for God, miracles do not necessarily indicate truthfulness. Moreover, many prophets (like Noah or Abraham, according to some accounts) were sent without miracles, yet they fulfilled their mission. So why do you insist on tying the prophet's truthfulness to miracles so tightly? The objection challenges the necessity of miracles as a condition for recognizing prophethood, especially given God's absolute freedom.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God must necessarily support every truthful prophet with miracles and cannot empower a liar with them.
Correction: If God can misguide, He could perform miracles at the hand of a liar, and many true prophets had no miracles. Therefore miracles are not a guaranteed sign of truthfulness.

Checkpoint

B) God cannot give miracles to liars.
C) If God can misguide, He could give miracles to a liar, so miracles are not a reliable test.
D) Miracles are the only way to know a prophet's truthfulness.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection shows that if God can misguide, miracles are not a necessary proof of prophethood because God could give them to a liar.

?

Fifth Objection: Are Miracles the Only Proof of Prophethood?
Fifth objection: alternative ways to know prophets

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection to miracles as the exclusive proof of prophethood and the dilemma it poses.

Core Idea
The objection forces a choice: restrict God or accept alternative proofs.

This lesson presents a key objection from theological opponents. They ask: why must miracles be the only way to identify true prophets? Could Allah have provided other signs? The objection presents a dilemma for those who insist on miracles exclusively.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two-part structure: a question followed by two conditional consequences.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection sets up a choice: either you deny any other way (first horn) or you allow it (second horn). Each leads to a problem.
First horn: 'if you deny... that is incapacitation.' Second horn: 'if you allow... why not obligatory?'
3
Understand the charge of 'incapacitation'
The first horn claims that denying other ways limits Allah's freedom—an act of 'ta'jiz' (incapacitation). Reflect on why this is seen as problematic.
Ta'jiz implies that the opponent is dictating to Allah how He must act.
4
Consider the challenge to necessity
The second horn asks: if other ways are possible, why is the miracle obligatory? What makes it more deserving? This challenges the unique status of miracles as proof.
The objection does not answer the question but leaves it as a challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قولهم : هل للخلق طريق إلى معرفة صدق الرسل سوى المعجزات الخارقة للعادات ؟ فإن منعتم ذلك فقد حصرتم القول وحسمتم الطريق على الله تعالى وذلك تعجيز وإن جوزتم ذلك فهلا وجب ذلك وجوب المعجزة، فما الذي جعل المعجزة أولى بالدلالة من ذلك الدليل؟
Among them is their statement: Is there for creation a way to know the truthfulness of messengers other than miracles that break customs? If you deny that, then you have restricted the statement and cut off the way for Allah, and that is incapacitation. And if you allow that, then why is that not obligatory as the miracle is obligatory? What makes the miracle more deserving of indication than that proof?
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection. It asks whether there is any other method for creation to know the truthfulness of messengers besides miraculous signs that break natural customs. If you deny any other method, then you have restricted God's options and 'cut off the way' for Him, which is an attempt to incapacitate God. If you allow that there is another method (such as rational arguments or moral character), then why is that method not equally obligatory as a miracle? In other words, what makes a miracle more worthy as evidence than that alternative? The objection challenges the exclusivity of miracles as a proof of prophethood, suggesting that other avenues might be equally valid or even preferable.

Key Terms
خلق
صدق الرسل
معجزات خارقة للعادات
حصر
تعجيز
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That miracles are the only possible way for God to demonstrate a prophet's truthfulness.
Correction: The objection does not deny that miracles are a valid proof, but questions whether they are the exclusive or uniquely necessary proof. It argues that if alternative methods exist, they should be equally valid.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim happens if one denies any way to know prophets besides miracles?
A) The denier is being too strict.
B) The denier restricts God and commits incapacitation.
C) The denier is actually agreeing with the objector.
D) The denier must produce another proof.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection challenges the exclusive necessity of miracles by presenting a dilemma: denying alternatives restricts God, while allowing them weakens miracles' unique status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a theological dilemma that seems to force a choice between two positions, do you rush to defend your familiar belief or do you honestly examine the challenge? Which response would Allah honor more?
?
The objectors claimed that denying alternative proofs 'cuts off the way for Allah'. Have you ever unconsciously limited God by assuming He must act only in specific ways? What would change if you truly believed His ways are beyond your categories?
?
If miracles were not the exclusive proof of prophethood, how would you personally know the truth of the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ? This lesson forces you to examine the foundation of your certainty—is it built on the miraculous or on a deeper conviction that has been tested?

The Miracle's Indication Cannot Be Nullified by Later Habituation
Refutation of the objection that later habit nullifies miracle indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a miracle's indication remains intact even if the extraordinary event later becomes habitual, and how the author refutes the objection of habituation.

Core Idea
Contextual evidence from the initial conjunction is not erased by later habit.

The author addresses a key objection: if a miracle later becomes habitual, does it lose its evidential force? He argues that the initial contextual evidence (qarina) established by the conjunction of the miracle with the claimant's challenge remains, and no later habit can remove it. He also notes that no prophetic miracle has ever been known to become habitual, so the objection is merely hypothetical.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument structure
The author first asserts that the miracle's indication comes from its conjunction with the claim (iqtiran). Even if the extraordinary event continues, the initial indication remains.
Notice the term 'iqtiran' (conjunction) is key.
2
Note the distinction between first occurrence and later habit
The author says habituation in the second state does not lift the first contextual evidence. The initial specification remains valid.
3
Recognize the author's additional support
Some scholars argue that miracles are also immune to habituation, as no prophetic miracle has become habitual later; the objection is only a rational hypothetical, which is countered by the above reasoning.
This reinforces the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل قصد المخصص بالوقت واختصاصه بدعواه دلیل قصد المخصص إلى التصديق، والوجهان حاصلان بنفس الاقتران، ولو توالى الخارق بعد ذلك لم ترتفع هذه الدلالة، فإن اعتياده في ثاني الحال لم يرفع القرينة الأولى ولا أبطل الاختصاص الأول ، وقال بعضهم: المعجزة كما سلمت عن المعارضة تسلم عن الاعتياد في ثاني الحال، ولهذا لم يعهد من معجزات الأنبياء ما كان خارقًا في الأول ثم صار معتادًا في الثاني الحال، وإنما هو إلزام تجويزي عقلي، فيندفع بما ذكرناه منعا للإلزام. وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثاني فهو يقارب ما ذكرناه من تحقيق وجه دلالة المعجزة، وبالجملة التجويزات الخيالية والوهمية مدفوعة بالدلائل العقلية والقرائن القطعية، وقد قررنا أن المعجزة ذات دلالة عقلية وهو اختصاصها بتحدي المدعي واقتضاء الاختصاص قصدًا إلى التخصيص، وجواز حصول مثل ذلك في قطر من الأقطار، أو في وقت من الأوقات لا تخرج الدلالة عن كونها دالة ؛ اقتران يدل على الاختصاص، واختصاص يدل على قصد لأنه لم يوجد التصديق. والمعجزة أيضًا ذات دلالة من حيث القرينة وجواز حصول مثله لا يرفع القرينة كمن عرف قطعًا بحكم قرينة اطراد العادة أن ماء الفرات لم ينقلب دما عبيطًا وهو جار جريانه في الأول، مع أنه يجوز عقلا أو وهما من قضية قدرة الله تعالى أنه قلبه دما جاريًا، أو يبس النهر عن الجريان وهذا التجويز لا يدفع العلم الضروري، وكذلك يجوز عقلا أن تكون الصفرة في الوجه غير دالة على الوجل، لكن إذا اقترنت بها حال وسبب للوجل علم ضرورة أنها صفرة الوجل لا صفرة المرض. ونقول: نعلم ضرورة أن الصعود إلى السماء والمشي على الماء وإحياء الموتى وقلب العصا حية تسعى وأمثال ذلك نقض لعادة البشر، فإذا اقترنت بتحدي الرسالة أو تصديق قول ما كانت آية وحجة على البشر، وإن لم تكن نقضا لعادة الملائكة والجن، والمعتبر في كون الآية حجة أن يكون ذلك نقضا لعادة كانت الآية حجة عليه والعادة عادة له، وكذلك لو ادعى النبي مدا وجزراً في جيحون كان ذلك حجة ؛ لأنه نقض لعادتها وإن كان معتادا لأهل البصرة، وكذلك لو قال آية صدقي أن ينبت الله نخيلا بخراسان، لكان ذلك آية معجزة له
The proof of the intention of the one specifying to the time and his specification to his claim — a proof of the intention of the one specifying to affirmation, and both aspects obtain by the conjunction itself, and if the extraordinary event were to follow afterwards, this indication would not be removed. For its becoming habitual in the second state did not lift the first contextual evidence nor invalidate the first specification. And some of them said: The miracle, just as it remains safe from counter-evidence, remains safe from becoming habitual in the second state, and for this reason it has not been known of the miracles of the prophets that what was extraordinary at first then became habitual in the second state. It is only a rational hypothetical objection, and it is repelled by what we mentioned as a prevention of the objection. As for the answer to the second question, it is close to what we mentioned of verifying the aspect of the miracle's indication. In summary, the imaginary and fanciful possibilities are repelled by rational proofs and certain contextual indicators. And we have established that the miracle possesses rational indication, which is its specificity to the challenge of the claimant and the requirement of specificity indicating intention to specify. And the possibility of something similar occurring in some region or at some time does not remove the indication from being indicative: conjunction indicates specificity, and specificity indicates intention, because the affirmation did not exist. And the miracle also possesses indication from the aspect of contextual evidence, and the possibility of something similar occurring does not lift the contextual evidence, just as one knows for certain by virtue of the contextual evidence of the regularity of habit that the water of the Euphrates did not turn into thick blood while it was flowing in its first flow, even though it is possible rationally or by fancy, as a matter of God's power, that He turned it into flowing blood, or dried the river from flowing. And this possibility does not repel necessary knowledge. Similarly, it is possible rationally that the paleness of the face is not indicative of fear, but when there is associated with it a state and cause for fear, it is known necessarily that it is the paleness of fear, not the paleness of illness. And we say: We know necessarily that ascending to heaven, walking on water, reviving the dead, and turning the staff into a moving serpent, and the like, are violations of human habit. So when they are associated with a challenge of prophethood or affirmation of a statement, they become a sign and proof upon humans, even if they are not a violation of the habit of the angels and jinn. And what matters in the sign being a proof is that it be a violation of a habit — the sign is a proof upon him, and the habit is his habit. Similarly, if a prophet claimed ebb and flow in the Jihun, that would be a proof, because it is a violation of its habit, even if it is habitual for the people of Basra. Similarly, if he said, 'My sign of truthfulness is that God grows palm trees in Khurasan,' that would be a miraculous sign for him.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument in defense of the evidential force of miracles (mu'jizat) against two key objections: (1) that a miracle might lose its indicatory power if it later becomes habitual, and (2) that the mere logical possibility of similar occurrences negates the certainty of its indication. The author first establishes that the miracle’s indication arises from its conjunction (iqtiran) with the claimant’s challenge, showing intentional specification. Even if the extraordinary event later becomes habitual, the initial contextual evidence (qarina) remains intact. He refutes the first objection by noting that no prophetic miracle has ever been known to become habitual afterward, and that the objection is only a hypothetical rational imposition. For the second question, he argues that all imaginary possibilities are repelled by rational proofs and certain contextual indicators. The miracle possesses rational indication through its specificity to the challenge, and the possibility of similar events elsewhere does not undermine this specificity. He gives examples: even if it is logically possible for the Euphrates to turn to blood or for the river to dry up, we know necessarily that it did not, based on the regularity of habit conjoined with absence of cause. Similarly, paleness can be known necessarily as the paleness of fear when associated with a cause. He then extends this reasoning to classic miracles: ascension, walking on water, reviving the dead — these are violations of human habit, and when conjoined with a prophetic challenge, they become certain proofs, regardless of whether they violate the habits of angels or jinn. The key criterion is that the miracle violates the habit of the audience to whom it is presented, not some other group. Thus, a claim of tidal change on a river in Central Asia would be a miracle for its local audience, even if it is habitual for people of Basra. Similarly, a claim of growing palm trees in Khurasan would be a miracle there. The author emphasizes that the indication is based on contextual certainty (darura), not abstract possibility.

Key Terms
معجزة
دلالة
اعتياد
قرينة
اقتران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a miracle becomes habitual later, it loses its evidential force.
Correction: The initial contextual evidence from the conjunction remains, and the later habit does not remove it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what preserves the miracle's indication if the extraordinary event later becomes habitual?
A) The event becomes a new miracle each time.
B) The initial contextual evidence (qarina) from the conjunction remains.
C) The prophet renews the challenge every time.
D) The event stops being extraordinary.

Key Takeaway
The miracle's indication is based on its initial conjunction with the challenge, not on its permanence, so later habituation does not nullify it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded a sign from Allah, and when it came, you later dismissed it because it didn't continue? Does your faith depend on a string of ongoing miracles, or on the initial truth of the message? What would it take for you to accept a single sign as enough?
?
If you were a companion of a prophet and saw a miracle, and then later the same event became normal, would your certainty waver? Why do you think the author insists that the first evidence cannot be erased?
?
Are you waiting for a 'perfect' proof that is always extraordinary before you fully commit to Allah? What does this lesson teach you about the sufficiency of the first encounter with truth? Will you act on what you have already received?
