The Certainty of Miracle Indication Against Mere Logical Possibility
Refutation of the objection that logical possibility undermines miracle certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the concept of contextual certainty (darura) to defend miracles against objections of logical possibility.

Core Idea
Logical possibility does not negate certain knowledge based on contextual evidence and habitual regularity.

The second objection claims that since it is logically possible for similar events to occur anywhere, the miracle's indication becomes uncertain. The author refutes this by arguing that rational proofs and certain contextual indicators (qara'in qat'iyya) override imaginary possibilities. He illustrates with examples: the Euphrates not turning to blood, paleness indicating fear, and specific miracles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's main rebuttal
Imaginary possibilities are repelled by rational proofs and certain contextual indicators. The miracle possesses rational indication through its specificity to the challenge.
The author mentions 'tajwizat khayaliyya wa wahmiyya' are repelled.
2
Examine the examples given
The author gives two analogies: the Euphrates water not turning to blood (despite logical possibility), and the paleness of face indicating fear (despite possibility of other causes). These show that contextual evidence produces necessary knowledge.
3
Apply to miracles
Miracles like ascending to heaven, walking on water, reviving the dead are violations of human habit. When conjoined with a prophetic challenge, they become certain proofs, regardless of logical possibilities.
The criterion is that the miracle violates the habit of the audience.

And the possibility of something similar occurring in some region or at some time does not remove the indication from being indicative: conjunction indicates specificity, and specificity indicates intention, because the affirmation did not exist. And the miracle also possesses indication from the aspect of contextual evidence, and the possibility of something similar occurring does not lift the contextual evidence, just as one knows for certain by virtue of the contextual evidence of the regularity of habit that the water of the Euphrates did not turn into thick blood while it was flowing in its first flow, even though it is possible rationally or by fancy, as a matter of God's power, that He turned it into flowing blood, or dried the river from flowing. And this possibility does not repel necessary knowledge. Similarly, it is possible rationally that the paleness of the face is not indicative of fear, but when there is associated with it a state and cause for fear, it is known necessarily that it is the paleness of fear, not the paleness of illness. And we say: We know necessarily that ascending to heaven, walking on water, reviving the dead, and turning the staff into a moving serpent, and the like, are violations of human habit. So when they are associated with a challenge of prophethood or affirmation of a statement, they become a sign and proof upon humans, even if they are not a violation of the habit of the angels and jinn. And what matters in the sign being a proof is that it be a violation of a habit — the sign is a proof upon him, and the habit is his habit. Similarly, if a prophet claimed ebb and flow in the Jihun, that would be a proof, because it is a violation of its habit, even if it is habitual for the people of Basra. Similarly, if he said, 'My sign of truthfulness is that God grows palm trees in Khurasan,' that would be a miraculous sign for him.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a continuous argument in defense of the evidential force of miracles (mu'jizat) against two key objections: (1) that a miracle might lose its indicatory power if it later becomes habitual, and (2) that the mere logical possibility of similar occurrences negates the certainty of its indication. The author first establishes that the miracle’s indication arises from its conjunction (iqtiran) with the claimant’s challenge, showing intentional specification. Even if the extraordinary event later becomes habitual, the initial contextual evidence (qarina) remains intact. He refutes the first objection by noting that no prophetic miracle has ever been known to become habitual afterward, and that the objection is only a hypothetical rational imposition. For the second question, he argues that all imaginary possibilities are repelled by rational proofs and certain contextual indicators. The miracle possesses rational indication through its specificity to the challenge, and the possibility of similar events elsewhere does not undermine this specificity. He gives examples: even if it is logically possible for the Euphrates to turn to blood or for the river to dry up, we know necessarily that it did not, based on the regularity of habit conjoined with absence of cause. Similarly, paleness can be known necessarily as the paleness of fear when associated with a cause. He then extends this reasoning to classic miracles: ascension, walking on water, reviving the dead — these are violations of human habit, and when conjoined with a prophetic challenge, they become certain proofs, regardless of whether they violate the habits of angels or jinn. The key criterion is that the miracle violates the habit of the audience to whom it is presented, not some other group. Thus, a claim of tidal change on a river in Central Asia would be a miracle for its local audience, even if it is habitual for people of Basra. Similarly, a claim of growing palm trees in Khurasan would be a miracle there. The author emphasizes that the indication is based on contextual certainty (darura), not abstract possibility.

Key Terms
تجويز
ضرورة
قرينة
اعتياد
تحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If it is logically possible for a similar event to occur elsewhere, the miracle's indication becomes uncertain.
Correction: Contextual evidence and necessary knowledge based on habit override mere logical possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the logical possibility that the Euphrates could turn to blood not affect our certain knowledge that it did not?
A) Because God does not perform such acts.
B) Because the contextual evidence of habitual regularity gives us certain knowledge.
C) Because the river is not under human control.
D) Because it would contradict divine wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Miracles produce certain knowledge because they are contextual violations of habit conjoined with a prophetic challenge, and mere logical possibility does not affect that certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter clear signs of Allah's power—whether in the Quran or in your life—do you allow 'what ifs' (logical possibilities) to create doubt, or do you surrender to the certainty that context and evidence demand? What would it cost you to stop hiding behind mere possibilities and submit to the truth?
?
The author shows that a miracle is a proof only for the people whose habitual experience it violates. In your own life, have you become so accustomed to Allah's blessings that you no longer see them as signs pointing to Him? What regular pattern in your life might actually be a miracle that you have normalized?
?
If you knew with certainty that a clear sign from Allah is before you—like the miracle of the Quran—but you fear the implications for your lifestyle, would you still resist? What 'fanciful possibilities' do you cling to in order to avoid the clear truth that has already been made evident to you?

The Logic of Qarīnah in Miracles
Al-Ash'ari explains that possibility does not remove the qarīnah of miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of a similar event occurring does not remove the contextual clue (qarīnah) that makes a miracle evident.

Core Idea
Possibility does not destroy certainty; custom provides a strong contextual clue.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari lays down a foundational principle: the evidential force of a miracle does not depend on its being impossible in itself, but on the contextual clue (qarīnah) provided by the consistency of custom. Even if something is rationally possible, the custom of how things normally are gives us necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Ash'ari builds his argument.
The passage uses analogies to clarify the concept of qarīnah.
2
Understand the Euphrates analogy
Al-Ash'ari gives the example of the Euphrates: we know with certainty that its water has not turned into blood, even though it is possible for God to do so. The consistency of custom is the qarīnah that gives us certainty.
The possibility of the event does not weaken that certainty.
3
Reflect on the yellowness example
Similarly, yellowness in the face can indicate fear or illness. When accompanied by a cause of fear, the qarīnah makes it certain that it is fear, not illness.
This shows how context (qarīnah) removes ambiguity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعجزة أيضًا ذات دلالة من حيث القرينة وجواز حصول مثله لا يرفع القرينة كمن عرف قطعًا بحكم قرينة اطراد العادة أن ماء الفرات لم ينقلب دما عبيطا وهو جار جريانه في الأول، مع أنه يجوز عقلا أو وهما من قضية قدرة الله تعالى أنه قلبه دما جاريًا، أو يبس النهر عن الجريان وهذا التجويز لا يدفع العلم الضروري، وكذلك يجوز عقلا أن تكون الصفرة في الوجه غير دالة على الوجل، لكن إذا اقترنت بها حال وسبب للوجل علم ضرورة أنها صفرة الوجل لا صفرة المرض. ونقول: نعلم ضرورة أن الصعود إلى السماء والمشي على الماء وإحياء الموتى وقلب العصا حية تسعى وأمثال ذلك نقض لعادة البشر، فإذا اقترنت بتحدي الرسالة أو تصديق قول ما كانت آية وحجة على البشر، وإن لم تكن نقضا لعادة الملائكة والجن، والمعتبر في كون الآية حجة أن يكون ذلك نقضا لعادة المخاطب، مثاله أن النبي لو ادعى أن جيحون له مد وجزر، فإذا جرى جيحون على عادته في المد والجزر، كانت الآية حجة عليه والعادة عادة له، وكذلك لو ادعى النبي مدا وجزراً في جيحون كان ذلك حجة؛ لأنه نقض لعادتها وإن كان معتادا لأهل البصرة، وكذلك لو قال آية صدقي أن ينبت الله نخيلا بخراسان، لكان ذلك آية معجزة له.
The miracle (mu'jizah) also has indication by way of contextual clue (qarīnah), and the possibility of its like occurring does not remove the contextual clue, like one who knows with certainty by the rule of the consistency of custom (iṭirād al-ʿādah) that the water of the Euphrates has not turned into thick blood while it is flowing as it flowed in the beginning, although it is rationally possible or hypothetically [possible] from the decree of God's power that He turned it into flowing blood, or that the river dried up from flowing, and this possibility does not repel necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī). And likewise, it is rationally possible that yellowness in the face does not indicate fear (al-wajal), but when a state and cause of fear are conjoined to it, one knows necessarily that it is the yellowness of fear, not the yellowness of illness. And we say: We know necessarily that ascending to the sky, walking on water, reviving the dead, turning the staff into a slithering snake, and the like of that are a breaking of the custom (naqḍ li-ʿādat) of humans. So if they are accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī) of prophethood or the affirmation of a statement, they become a sign (āyah) and proof (ḥujjah) against humans, even if they are not a breaking of the custom of angels and jinn. And what is considered in the sign being a proof is that it be a breaking of the custom of the addressee. Example: if the prophet claims that the Oxus has ebb and flow, then if the Oxus flows according to its custom in ebb and flow, the sign becomes a proof against him and the custom is his custom. And likewise, if the prophet claims ebb and flow in the Oxus, that becomes a proof because it is a breaking of its custom, even if it is customary for the people of Basra. And likewise, if he says, 'The sign of my truthfulness is that God makes date palms grow in Khurasan,' that would be a miraculous sign (āyah muʿjizah) for him.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage defines the evidential nature of miracles and the condition for them to be proofs. It begins by explaining that the possibility of a similar event occurring does not nullify the contextual clue (qarīnah) that indicates the miracle's evidential value. It gives an analogy: we know with certainty that the water of the Euphrates has not turned into blood, even though it is rationally possible for God to change it, because the consistency of custom provides certainty. Similarly, facial yellowness can be ambiguous, but when combined with a known cause of fear, we know it is the yellowness of fear. Then the author states that we know necessarily that acts like ascending to the sky, walking on water, reviving the dead, and turning a staff into a snake are breaks of human custom. If these acts are accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī) to prove prophethood or to affirm a statement, they become a sign and proof against humans, even if they do not break the custom of angels or jinn. The key condition is that the sign must break the custom of the addressee, not of all beings. The author gives examples: if a prophet claims the Oxus has ebb and flow, and it actually does, that would not be a miracle for the people of that region if it is customary for them; but if the prophet claims it has ebb and flow when it does not, and it then does, that breaks the custom of the river and becomes a proof, even if it is customary elsewhere. Similarly, growing date palms in Khurasan would be a miracle because it breaks the custom of that land.

معجزة
قرينة
اطراد العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mere possibility of a similar event invalidates the certainty from custom.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that possibility does not remove the contextual clue; custom can still provide necessary knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do we know with certainty that the Euphrates water has not turned into blood?
A) Because it is impossible for water to become blood
B) Because the consistency of custom provides a contextual clue
C) Because we have seen it turn into blood before
D) Because prophets have told us

Key Takeaway
The consistency of custom provides necessary knowledge that is not undermined by mere rational possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness clear patterns of Allah's mercy and power in your life, do you let the thought 'but it could be otherwise' weaken your certainty? What would it take for you to embrace the qarīnah of His custom as sufficient proof?
?
In moments of trial, do you look for the 'yellowness of fear' and allow the context of your faith to interpret it, or do you default to the 'yellowness of illness' and despair? How can you train your heart to see the qarīnah of divine wisdom in your circumstances?
?
The passage shows that necessary knowledge comes from consistent custom. Is your relationship with Allah based on a ‘custom’ of daily reflection and gratitude, or do you rely on occasional moments of intensity? What consistent habit will you build today to strengthen your certainty in His signs?

Miracles

Defining Miracle: Breaking Custom with Challenge
Al-Ash'ari states the condition for a miracle being a proof: breaking the addressee's custom with challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a miracle is a break of the addressee's custom accompanied by a challenge, and that it does not need to break the custom of all beings.

Core Idea
A miracle is a sign from God only when it breaks the custom of the people it is meant to convince.

Al-Ash'ari now defines more precisely what constitutes a miracle (mu'jizah). It must be a break of human custom, but only of the addressee's custom, and it must be accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī). Even if angels or jinn normally do such acts, it is still a miracle for humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the list of actions (ascending to sky, walking on water, etc.) and the condition of challenge.
Notice that these actions are described as breaks of human custom.
2
Understand the condition of challenge
The passage states that these breaks become signs and proofs only when accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī) to prophethood or affirmation of a statement.
Without challenge, they are just unusual events.
3
Grasp the addressee's custom condition
Al-Ash'ari emphasizes that the proof lies in breaking the custom of the addressee, not of all beings. He gives examples of the Oxus and date palms in Khurasan to illustrate.
This clarifies that a miracle is relative to the audience.

How Miracles Remove Doubt for Experts and Common People
Al-Ash'ari answers how miracles remove doubt for all people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how miracles remove doubt for different groups (experts and common people) and why individual miracles are not required for every person.

Core Idea
Miracles remove doubt through expert verification and widespread transmission, not individual demonstrations.

Al-Ash'ari now addresses the third objection: How can a single miracle convince everyone? He explains that miracles first remove doubt for experts in trickery, then through their testimony and widespread transmission, others are convinced. The prophet is not required to perform a miracle for each individual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Al-Ash'ari structures his answer: first the principle, then experts, then common people, then transmission.
2
Identify the key groups
The passage mentions several groups: the challenger (prophet), people of expertise, people of other crafts, the most severe deniers (experts in trickery), common people, and others who hear via transmission. Identify each and their role.
3
Understand the logic for experts
Al-Ash'ari says that experts in trickery are the hardest to convince, but if a miracle appears contrary to their expectations, they become the most accepting. Then their certainty spreads to others.
This is a key point: the miracle targets those most likely to detect fraud.
4
Understand the logic for common people
Common people may initially doubt due to illusions, but when they hear the clear testimony of experts, they must accept without hesitation. Miracles don't need to be shown to everyone individually.
5
Reflect on the role of transmission
The passage explains that once the prophet's truthfulness is established, his message reaches others via widespread and mutawātir transmission, whether during his time or after.
This shows that miracles have a lasting effect through reliable reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن السؤال الثالث أن نقول : كل من عارضه شك في صدق المتحدي وجب إراحته، لكن المعجزة إذا ظهرت لأهل الخبرة وحصل لهم العلم بذلك وهو جم غفير فأخرى أن لا يبقى شك لأهل الصناعات الأخر، وإن أشد الناس إنكارا من كان عنده خبرة وبصيرة بخواص الأشياء وأحوال أهل المخرقة والتخيل، وإذا ظهرت المعجزة على خلاف ما توهموه فهو أولى الناس بالقبول. وأما العامة فالتخيلات والمخاريق إذا أورثت لهم شكا في الحال، فأولى أن لا يعتريهم ريب في مقال أصحاب المعجزات فيلزمهم الإقرار بتصديقه، ويلزم غيرهم من العامة إذا سمعوا منهم جلية الأمر الاعتراف به من غير توقف ولا ريب، ولا يجب على النبي عليه السلام تتبع آحاد الناس وإفراد كل واحد بمعجزة خاصة، وإذا تقرر صدقه فنسبة قوله إلى من صح عنده كنسبته إلى غيره؛ إما في زمانه وهو في قطر آخر باستفاضة الخبر إليه والمواترة عليه، وإما في غير زمانه بالنقل والاستفاضة والشیوع.
And as for the answer to the third question, we say: Everyone who is faced with doubt concerning the truthfulness of the challenger must be relieved [of that doubt]. But when the miracle appears to the people of expertise (ahl al-khibrah) and they obtain knowledge of it, and they are a great multitude, then it is more fitting that no doubt remain for the people of other crafts. And the most severely denying people are those who have expertise and insight (baṣīrah) into the properties of things and the conditions of the people of trickery (al-makhraqah) and illusion (al-takhayyul). When the miracle appears contrary to what they had supposed, then he is the most deserving of people for acceptance. And as for the common people (al-ʿāmmah), if illusions and trickeries give rise to doubt in them at the moment, then it is more fitting that no doubt befall them concerning the statement of the people of miracles, so they are obligated to acknowledge his truthfulness. And it is necessary for others of the common people, when they hear from them the clear matter, to acknowledge it without hesitation or doubt. And it is not incumbent upon the prophet, peace be upon him, to follow each individual and single out every one with a specific miracle. And when his truthfulness is established, then the relationship of his statement to one for whom it is correctly transmitted is like its relationship to another; either in his time while he is in a different region through the widespread transmission (istifāḍah) of the report to him and its mutawātir transmission, or in other than his time through transmission (naql), widespread transmission (istifāḍah), and commonality (shuyūʿ).
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage answers the third question regarding how miracles remove doubt. The author states that anyone who is in doubt about the truthfulness of the challenger must be relieved of that doubt. When a miracle appears to experts (ahl al-khibrah) who have knowledge of the properties of things and the conditions of trickery and illusion, and they are a great multitude, then it is even more fitting that no doubt remains for people of other crafts. The most severe deniers are those with expertise and insight into trickery and illusion; when a miracle appears contrary to what they had assumed, they are the most likely to accept it. As for common people, even if illusions and trickeries cause them doubt at first, it is even more fitting that they have no doubt about the statements of miracle-workers, so they are obligated to acknowledge the prophet's truthfulness. If they hear from the experts the clear truth, they must accept without hesitation. The prophet is not required to show a specific miracle to each individual. Once his truthfulness is established, his words reach others through widespread and mutawātir transmission, whether in his time in other regions or after his time through reliable transmission and common knowledge.

Key Terms
خبرة
بصيرة
مخرقة
تخيل
استفاضة
مواترة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That common people need to witness a miracle individually to be convinced.
Correction: The passage states that common people can rely on expert testimony and widespread transmission; individual miracles are not required.

Key Takeaway
Miracles remove doubt through expert verification and widespread transmission, not by individual demonstrations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there ever been a time when you demanded a personal, direct sign from God before you would believe, rather than accepting the testimony of reliable scholars and the transmitted miracles of the prophets? What does that reveal about your heart?
?
When you hear about the miracles of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) through transmitted reports, do you treat them as certain knowledge, or do you harbor doubts that you would not have if you had seen them yourself? How can you train your soul to accept the testimony of the experts and the mutawātir reports?
?
If you were a severe denier—someone expert in the ways of trickery and illusion—and then encountered a true miracle, would you be humble enough to admit you were wrong? Or would pride and prior knowledge prevent you from accepting the truth? What changes must you make now to ensure your heart remains open to divine signs?

Miracles

Miracles and the Meaning of 'Isma
Al-Shahrastani classifies miracles into specific/general and defines 'isma as continuous indication of truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of miracles into specific and general, and how the continuous indication of truthfulness defines prophetic infallibility ('isma).

Core Idea
Prophetic infallibility is the uninterrupted miracle of truthfulness.

Al-Shahrastani begins by distinguishing two types of miracles: those that confirm a specific claim and those that confirm the prophet's overall truthfulness. This distinction leads to a crucial definition: prophetic infallibility is not an arbitrary protection but the logical consequence of the continuous presence of the miracle. In this lesson, we will unpack his argument and see why 'isma is necessary for prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Shahrastani uses a very systematic classification.
2
Identify the two types of miracles
Find the two categories: specific miracles (ayat makhsusa) and general miracles (ayat 'amma). Notice how each type functions in relation to the prophet's claim.
General miracles can be verbal or physical—look for examples.
3
Understand the definition of 'isma
The passage concludes that the continuous indication of truthfulness is exactly what 'isma means. Ask yourself: why must this indication never be interrupted?
This connects to the necessity of 'isma for the credibility of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن الآيات قد تكون آيات مخصوصة على كل مسألة وقول يدعي ذلك، وذلك مثل دلالته على التوحيد بأن الإله واحد في خلقه وأمره لا شريك له. وقد تكون الآيات عامة تدل على صدق قوله في جميع أقواله وأحواله، وتلك الآيات قد تكون من جنس الأقوال كآيات الكتاب وقد تكون من جنس الأفعال كآيات الإحياء وقلب الجماد حيوانًا، وبالجملة فدلالة الصدق لا تنفك عن حالة ومقالة طرفة عين، وذلك هو المعنى بالعصمة الواجبة للأنبياء عليهم السلام؛ لأن العصمة لو ارتفعت بطلت الدلالة وتناقضت الدعوى، خصوصا فيما أرسلوا به إليهم وكلف الناس تصديقه في أقواله ومتابعته في أفعاله.
But signs (ayat) may be specific signs (ayat makhsusa) for each issue and statement claimed, such as his indication of tawhid that the deity is one in His creation and command, no partner for Him. And signs may be general ('amma), indicating the truth of his statement in all his statements and states. And those signs may be of the type of statements, like the verses of the Book (ayat al-kitab), and may be of the type of actions, like the signs of giving life and turning an inanimate object into an animal. In sum, the indication of truthfulness (dalalat al-sidq) is never separated from a state or a statement for the blink of an eye. And that is the meaning of the infallibility ('isma) obligatory for the prophets, peace be upon them. Because if infallibility were lifted, the indication would be invalid and the claim would be contradictory, especially in what they were sent with to them and the people are obligated to believe in his statements and follow his actions.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage classifies miracles (ayat) into two types: specific and general. Specific miracles confirm a particular claim made by the prophet, e.g., a miracle demonstrating the truth of his tawhid teaching that God is one. General miracles confirm the prophet's overall truthfulness in all his statements and states. General miracles can be verbal, like the Qur'an, or physical, like bringing the dead to life or transforming objects. The author then draws the crucial conclusion: the indication of truthfulness (dalalat al-sidq) is never absent from the prophet, even for a moment. This uninterrupted indication is precisely what 'isma (infallibility) means for prophets. The necessity of 'isma is argued: if it were removed, the evidential force of the miracle would collapse and the prophetic claim would become contradictory. This is especially important since people are required to believe and follow the prophet in all things.

Key Terms
آيات مخصوصة
آيات عامة
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'isma is an arbitrary divine protection that prevents prophets from sinning.
Correction: In this passage, 'isma is defined as the logical corollary of the continuous presence of the miracle—the indication of truthfulness never leaves the prophet, which is the basis of infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'isma (infallibility) for prophets?
A) That they never commit any sin
B) That they are always in a state of ritual purity
C) That the indication of truthfulness never leaves them
D) That they have knowledge of the unseen

Key Takeaway
Prophetic infallibility ('isma) is the uninterrupted divine indication of truthfulness, not an arbitrary protection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your conviction that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is truthful in every statement and action shape how you speak and act today, or do you separate his example from your daily decisions? Will you commit to asking 'What would the Prophet say or do?' before every major choice this week?
?
If the indication of truthfulness is never absent from the Prophet for even a blink of an eye, what does that mean for the moments you doubt or selectively follow his teachings? Are you willing to treat every prophetic teaching as a continuous miracle demanding your full obedience, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The passage says infallibility is necessary because without it the claim would contradict itself. How does this logical necessity confront your own tendency to rationalize disobedience? Can you name one area where you have been inconsistent in following the Prophet's example, and will you resolve to change it today?

The Unparalleled Eloquence of the Qur'an
Author establishes the Qur'an's unparalleled eloquence and concludes it is divine revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an's unique linguistic style, unlike any Arab speech, proves its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's language is so distinct that it cannot be human speech.

The author begins by showing that the Qur'an is utterly unlike the Arabs' own literary productions. He makes a bold comparison: the gap between their best words and the Qur'an is greater than the gap between Arabic and all other languages. This sets the stage for the argument of miraculous inimitability.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening comparison
Notice how the author contrasts the Qur'an with Arab prose, poetry, rhymed prose, and verse. He claims the difference is immense.
This is a strong rhetorical claim.
2
Compare the Prophet's words
The author then compares the Prophet's own speech to the revealed Qur'an, saying they are as different as foot and crown.
3
Grasp the conclusion
From this, the author concludes that the Qur'an must be divine revelation, evidence of the Prophet's truthfulness.
This is the logical inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لم تعهده العرب في نظمهم ونثرهم وسجعهم وشعرهم، بحيث لو قوبل أفصح كلماتهم بسورة واحدة من القرآن كان التفاوت بينهما أكثر من التفاوت بين لسان العرب وبين سائر الألسن، ولو خاير بين كلمات النبي نفسه وبين ما نزل عليه من الكتاب المهيمن على الكتب كلها كان الفرق بينهما فرق ما بين القدم والفرق، والصرف بينهما صرف ما بين الولاية والصرف، فعلم ضرورة وقطعًا أن الذي جاء به وحي يوحى إليه، وتنزيل ينزل عليه دلالة له على صدقه ومعجزة له على فصحاء العرب وبلغاء أهل اللسان وشعراء ذلك الزمان، فتحدى بذلك تحدي التعجيز عن الإتيان بمثله كتاباً وقرآنًا: ( قُلْ فَأْتُوا بِكِتَبٍ مِنْ عِندِ اللَّهِ هُوَ أَهْدَى مِنْهُمَا ﴾ [القصص: ١٤٩]، ثم ثنى ذلك فقال: ( فأتوا بحديث مثله )، ولما عجزوا عن الإتيان بمثله نزل عن ذلك حين قال القوم: إنه افتراه، فقال: ﴿ قُلْ فَأْتُوا بِعَشْرِ سُوَرٍ مِثْلِهِ مُفْتَرَيَات ﴾ [هود: ١٣] ونزل عن [البقرة: ٢٣]. ولما ظهر عجزهم وبان خزيهم أنذرهم صاحب التنزيل وحذرهم المتحدي بالدليل: ﴿ فَإِن لَّمْ تَفْعَلُوا وَلَن تَفْعَلُوا فَاتَّقُوا النَّارَ الَّتِي وَقُودُهَا النَّاسُ وَالْحِجَارَةُ أُعِدَّتْ لِلْكَافِرِينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٤]، وكيف لا يكون التعجيز ظاهرا ظهور الشمس والكتاب يقرأ عليهم: ﴿ وَرَبُّكَ أَعْلَمُ بِمَن فِي السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَلَقَدْ فَضَّلْنَا بَعْضَ النَّبِينَ عَلَى بَعْضٍ وَآتَيْنَا دَاوُدَ زَبُورًا ﴾ [الإسراء: ٥٥] الآية، أفلم يكن بليغ من بلغاء العرب وفصيح من فصحائهم يتصدى لمعارضة هذه الدعوى العريضة الواسعة لنوعي الجن والإنس وقد خيروا بين معارضة القرآن والخروج بالسيف والسنان فكيف اختاروا الأشد على الأضعف، وآثروا ما فيه بذل المهج والنفوس واستحلال النساء واسترقاق الأولاد واستباحة الأموال على أهون الأمور وأيسر ما في المقدور وهو معارضة سورة واحدة. أفلا يكون ذلك عجزاً ظاهراً ونكولا واضحا، وإن استراب في ذلك مستريب تشكيكا لنفسه في مظان القطع واليقين، وتسييعا للغزالة بالطين؟ فالنقد حاضر لم يبدل، ولسان القرآن على رأس التحدي، كما ورد في الخبر القرآن حي يجري كما يجري الليل والنهار ولسان المعارض عي عن المعارضة من مدة خمس مائة سنة وزيادة، وبلغاء العرب قد اجتمعت لهم متانة الشعر في العصر الأول، ولطافة الطبع من العصر الأخير، أولم يفدنا عجز الأولين والآخرين دلالة ظاهرة على أن مضمون القرآن حق، وأن قول من أتى به صدق؟ كيف ولو اعتبرت سورة بسورة اعتبار متأمل فيها منصف صادف كل سورة على حيالها اشتملت على صنف من
What the Arabs were not accustomed to in their prose, poetry, rhymed prose, and verse, such that if the most eloquent of their words were compared to a single surah of the Qur'an, the disparity between them would be greater than the disparity between the Arabic tongue and all other tongues. And if the words of the Prophet himself were compared to that which was revealed to him from the Book that dominates all books, the difference between them would be the difference between the foot and the crown [i.e., incomparable], and the distance between them would be the distance between wilayah and sarf [i.e., immense]. So it was known necessarily and decisively that what he brought was revelation inspired to him and a revelation sent down upon him, as evidence of his truthfulness and a miracle for him against the eloquent of the Arabs, the skilled speakers, and the poets of that time. So he challenged them with a challenge of incapacitation to bring something like it as a book and a Qur'an: 'Say, then bring a book from Allah which is better guiding than both of them' [Qasas 28:49]. Then he repeated that saying: 'Bring a narration like it.' And when they were unable to bring something like it, he descended from that when the people said he had fabricated it, and he said: 'Say, then bring ten surahs like it fabricated' [Hud 11:13]. And he descended from ten to one surah like it, and Allah Almighty said: 'And if you are in doubt about what We have revealed to Our servant, then bring a surah like it and call your witnesses besides Allah, if you are truthful' [Baqarah 2:23]. And when their inability appeared and their disgrace became clear, the owner of the revelation warned them and the challenger cautioned them with the proof: 'But if you do not—and you will never do it—then fear the fire whose fuel is men and stones, prepared for the disbelievers' [Baqarah 2:24]. How could the incapacitation not be as clear as the sun while the book is recited to them: 'And your Lord knows best who is in the heavens and the earth, and indeed We have preferred some prophets over others, and We gave David the Psalms' [Isra 17:55]. Was there not an eloquent Arab and a fluent speaker among them who would rise to oppose this vast and wide claim to both humankind and jinn, when they were given the choice between opposing the Qur'an and going out with sword and spear? So how did they choose the harder over the easier, and prefer what involves sacrifice of lives, souls, allowance of women, enslavement of children, and plunder of wealth, over the easiest and most accessible of possible things, namely opposing one surah? Is that not clear inability and obvious failure? Even if a doubter doubts that, causing himself to be skeptical in matters of certainty and assurance, is he throwing mud at the sun? So the coin of proof is present and unchanged, and the tongue of the Qur'an is at the peak of challenge, as has come in the report: 'The Qur'an is alive, running as the night and day run.' And the tongue of the opponent is unable to oppose it for a period of five hundred years and more. And the eloquent Arabs combined for them the solid poetry of the first age and the refined nature of the later age. Does the inability of the earlier and later people not give us clear indication that the content of the Qur'an is true, and that the statement of the one who brought it is truthful? How? And if one surah is considered by the consideration of a just pondering person, he would find each surah on its own contains a kind of...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) of prophethood through the miracle of the Qur'an's inimitability (i‘jaz). The author begins by stating that the Qur'an is unlike anything the Arabs had ever produced in any literary form—prose, poetry, rhymed prose, or verse—so much so that the disparity between their most eloquent words and a single surah is far greater than the disparity between the Arabic tongue and all other languages. He even claims that the difference between the Prophet's own words and the revealed Book is immense. Thus it is known with necessity and certainty that what the Prophet brought is divine revelation, serving as proof of his truthfulness and as a miracle against the eloquent Arabs and poets of that time. The Prophet challenged them progressively: first to bring a book better than the Qur'an (Qasas 28:49), then to bring a single narration like it, then ten fabricated surahs (Hud 11:13), and finally just one surah (Baqarah 2:23). When they failed, Allah warned them of hellfire (Baqarah 2:24). The author rhetorically asks why the Arabs, given the choice between opposing the Qur'an and facing war, chose war—the harder option—rather than the easier task of producing one surah. This proves their utter inability. The author concludes that if doubters still suspect, they are like someone throwing mud at the sun. The challenge remains, and the Qur'an's tongue continues to challenge, while the opponent's tongue has been unable to respond for over five hundred years. The cumulative failure of all Arabs, past and present, is a clear sign that the Qur'an is true and that Muhammad is truthful. The passage ends mid-sentence, transitioning to the idea that examining each surah reveals its miraculous nature.

الإعجاز
الفصاحة
البلاغة

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the difference between the Prophet's own words and the Quran?
A) They are similar
B) The Prophet's words are better
C) The difference is immense
D) There is no difference

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's unique linguistic style, unmatched by any Arab speech including the Prophet's own, indicates its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you sense that its words are utterly unlike any human speech, or have you become desensitized to its unique quality? What would change in your relationship with the Qur'an if you truly internalized this difference?
?
If the Prophet's own words could not match the Qur'an's eloquence, how can you justify treating any human writing—philosophy, poetry, or even your own thoughts—as comparable or superior to its guidance? Are there areas of your life where you prioritize other sources over the Qur'an?
?
The author says it is known 'necessarily and decisively' that the Qur'an is divine. Have you reached such certainty in your own heart? If not, what is holding you back—and what step will you take today to remove that barrier?

The Progressive Challenge (Tahaddi)
Author lists the three stages of the Qur'anic challenge and the warning that followed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the stages of the Qur'anic challenge to the Arabs and how their failure proves its inimitability.

Core Idea
The challenge to produce even one surah was the final proof of the Arabs' inability.

After establishing the Qur'an's uniqueness, the author recounts the prophet's challenge to the Arabs. The challenge was progressive: first a book, then ten surahs, then one surah. The Arabs' failure to meet even the smallest challenge is a clear sign of the Qur'an's miraculous nature.

Guided Path
1
Follow the descending challenge
Notice how the challenge starts with a whole book, then reduces to ten surahs, then to one surah. This escalation emphasizes the Arabs' incapacity.
Each stage is a response to their objections.
2
Observe the Quranic verses
Read the verses quoted (Qasas 28:49, Hud 11:13, Baqarah 2:23) to see the exact wording of the challenge.
3
Understand the ultimate warning
When the Arabs failed, Allah warned them with hellfire (Baqarah 2:24).
This reinforces the seriousness of disbelief.

What the Arabs were not accustomed to in their prose, poetry, rhymed prose, and verse, such that if the most eloquent of their words were compared to a single surah of the Qur'an, the disparity between them would be greater than the disparity between the Arabic tongue and all other tongues. And if the words of the Prophet himself were compared to that which was revealed to him from the Book that dominates all books, the difference between them would be the difference between the foot and the crown [i.e., incomparable], and the distance between them would be the distance between wilayah and sarf [i.e., immense]. So it was known necessarily and decisively that what he brought was revelation inspired to him and a revelation sent down upon him, as evidence of his truthfulness and a miracle for him against the eloquent of the Arabs, the skilled speakers, and the poets of that time. So he challenged them with a challenge of incapacitation to bring something like it as a book and a Qur'an: 'Say, then bring a book from Allah which is better guiding than both of them' [Qasas 28:49]. Then he repeated that saying: 'Bring a narration like it.' And when they were unable to bring something like it, he descended from that when the people said he had fabricated it, and he said: 'Say, then bring ten surahs like it fabricated' [Hud 11:13]. And he descended from ten to one surah like it, and Allah Almighty said: 'And if you are in doubt about what We have revealed to Our servant, then bring a surah like it and call your witnesses besides Allah, if you are truthful' [Baqarah 2:23]. And when their inability appeared and their disgrace became clear, the owner of the revelation warned them and the challenger cautioned them with the proof: 'But if you do not—and you will never do it—then fear the fire whose fuel is men and stones, prepared for the disbelievers' [Baqarah 2:24]. How could the incapacitation not be as clear as the sun while the book is recited to them: 'And your Lord knows best who is in the heavens and the earth, and indeed We have preferred some prophets over others, and We gave David the Psalms' [Isra 17:55]. Was there not an eloquent Arab and a fluent speaker among them who would rise to oppose this vast and wide claim to both humankind and jinn, when they were given the choice between opposing the Qur'an and going out with sword and spear? So how did they choose the harder over the easier, and prefer what involves sacrifice of lives, souls, allowance of women, enslavement of children, and plunder of wealth, over the easiest and most accessible of possible things, namely opposing one surah? Is that not clear inability and obvious failure? Even if a doubter doubts that, causing himself to be skeptical in matters of certainty and assurance, is he throwing mud at the sun? So the coin of proof is present and unchanged, and the tongue of the Qur'an is at the peak of challenge, as has come in the report: 'The Qur'an is alive, running as the night and day run.' And the tongue of the opponent is unable to oppose it for a period of five hundred years and more. And the eloquent Arabs combined for them the solid poetry of the first age and the refined nature of the later age. Does the inability of the earlier and later people not give us clear indication that the content of the Qur'an is true, and that the statement of the one who brought it is truthful? How? And if one surah is considered by the consideration of a just pondering person, he would find each surah on its own contains a kind of...
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) of prophethood through the miracle of the Qur'an's inimitability (i‘jaz). The author begins by stating that the Qur'an is unlike anything the Arabs had ever produced in any literary form—prose, poetry, rhymed prose, or verse—so much so that the disparity between their most eloquent words and a single surah is far greater than the disparity between the Arabic tongue and all other languages. He even claims that the difference between the Prophet's own words and the revealed Book is immense. Thus it is known with necessity and certainty that what the Prophet brought is divine revelation, serving as proof of his truthfulness and as a miracle against the eloquent Arabs and poets of that time. The Prophet challenged them progressively: first to bring a book better than the Qur'an (Qasas 28:49), then to bring a single narration like it, then ten fabricated surahs (Hud 11:13), and finally just one surah (Baqarah 2:23). When they failed, Allah warned them of hellfire (Baqarah 2:24). The author rhetorically asks why the Arabs, given the choice between opposing the Qur'an and facing war, chose war—the harder option—rather than the easier task of producing one surah. This proves their utter inability. The author concludes that if doubters still suspect, they are like someone throwing mud at the sun. The challenge remains, and the Qur'an's tongue continues to challenge, while the opponent's tongue has been unable to respond for over five hundred years. The cumulative failure of all Arabs, past and present, is a clear sign that the Qur'an is true and that Muhammad is truthful. The passage ends mid-sentence, transitioning to the idea that examining each surah reveals its miraculous nature.

Key Terms
التحدي
المعارضة
القرآن

Checkpoint

A) To bring a book
B) To bring ten surahs
C) To bring a single surah
D) To bring a narration

Key Takeaway
The progressive challenge demonstrates that the Arabs could not match even the smallest part of the Qur'an, proving its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Quran challenges you to produce even a single surah like it. Have you ever genuinely taken that challenge to heart, or do you assume its divinity without engaging with the evidence?
?
God declared the Arabs would never succeed, and history proved them unable. Yet many today treat the Quran as a human book. If you are convinced it is divine, does your daily life reflect that belief in submission and awe?
?
The warning of Hellfire is for those who reject after the proof is clear. Is there any area of your life where you are avoiding the implications of this truth? What will you change today to align your actions with your conviction?

The Historical Evidence: Arabs Chose War Over Challenge
Author uses historical fact of Arabs choosing war to prove the Qur'an's inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Arabs' choice to fight rather than produce a surah proves the Qur'an's miraculous nature.

Core Idea
The Arabs' preference for war over literary challenge shows their utter inability.

The author now presents a historical argument: the Arabs were given a clear choice between opposing the Qur'an with a similar composition or facing war. They chose the difficulty of war, which required sacrifice of lives and property, over the seemingly easier task of producing one surah. This is taken as clear evidence of their incapacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the rhetorical question
The author asks why the Arabs would choose the harder option if they could produce a surah. This is a powerful persuasive device.
The choice was real and historical.
2
Notice the contrast
The contrast is between the ease of literary production and the hardship of war. The fact that they chose war proves they could not produce the literary work.
3
Consider the cumulative proof
The author adds that the challenge has stood for over 500 years, and still no one has produced a comparable surah. This shows the ongoing nature of the miracle.
The Quranic 'coin' is still current.

Linguistic Inimitability of the Qur'an
Author argues for linguistic i'jāz via eloquence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'an's linguistic inimitability lies in its unique combination of eloquence, rhetoric, and fluency, which even the most eloquent Arabs could not imitate, as demonstrated by a key example.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's language is so unparalleled that Arabs called it 'magic'.

In this passage, the author argues for a specific aspect of the Qur'an's miraculous nature: its linguistic beauty. He points out that no surah matches another in its eloquence, and even when stories repeat, the expression differs in a way that cannot be replicated by humans. The reaction of the most eloquent Arabs—calling it 'magic'—serves as a powerful testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the key terms (fasāḥah, balāghah, jazālah). Try to absorb the rhythm and structure.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the Author's Argument
Identify the claim about the uniqueness of each surah and the example of the story of Moses in two different surahs. Notice how the author says the expression differs without harming the meaning.
3
Analyze the Rhetorical Question
The author asks 'Who can do the like from humans?' Consider why this is a compelling proof of divine origin.
The rhetorical question forces the reader to admit human inability.
4
Review the Arab Eloquents' Response
They said it is 'magic that is transmitted'. Reflect on why this admission from the most skilled poets is significant.
Even enemies acknowledged its supernatural quality.
5
Connect to Broader I'jāz
Think about how linguistic inimitability fits into the overall miracle of the Qur'an and why it is a central proof in Islamic theology.
This lesson focuses on wording; other aspects include content and predictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصاحة وضرب من البلاغة والجزالة ولم تشاركها في ذلك سورة أخرى، وربما تكون القصة واحدة والتعبير عنها مختلف اختلافا لا يخل بالمعاني، ويكون أسلوب النظم والبلاغة نوعا آخر يباين الأولى، واختبر هذا المعنى في قصة مثناة، وبالخصوص في قصة موسى عليه السلام في سورة المص، وقصته في سورة طه، فمن ذا الذي يقدر على مثل ذلك من البشر ؟ وكيف يصل إلى غايته القوة المنطقية الإنسانية، ولهذا لما فطنت البلغاء من العرب البديع النظم والجزالة فيه قالوا : ( إنّ هَذَا إِلَّا سِحْرٌ يُؤْثَرُ ﴾ [المدثر : ٢٤] هذا من حيث اللفظ.
Eloquence and a kind of rhetoric and fluency, and no other surah shares with it in that. And perhaps the story is one and the expression about it differs in a difference that does not harm the meanings, and the style of composition and eloquence becomes another type that differs from the first. And this meaning was tested in a repeated story, and especially in the story of Moses (peace be upon him) in Surat al-Sad, and his story in Surat Ta-Ha. So who is there that can do the like of that from humans? And how can the human logical faculty reach its utmost? And for this reason, when the eloquent ones among the Arabs perceived the wonderful composition and fluency in it, they said: 'This is nothing but magic that is transmitted.' [al-Muddaththir: 24] This is with respect to the wording.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the linguistic inimitability (i'jāz) of the Qur'an. The author begins by stating that the Qur'an possesses a unique combination of eloquence (fasāḥah), rhetoric (balāghah), and fluency (jazālah). No other surah matches it in these qualities. Even when the same story appears in different surahs, the expression differs in a way that does not harm the meaning but produces a distinct and superior style. The author gives the example of the story of Moses in Surat al-Sad and Surat Ta-Ha. He then asks rhetorically: what human being can produce such a thing? The human logical faculty cannot reach that level. He notes that even the most eloquent Arabs, upon perceiving the wonderful composition and fluency of the Qur'an, admitted that it was not human speech but 'magic that is transmitted' (sihr yu'aththar). This concludes the argument regarding the wording (al-lafẓ).

فصاحة
بلاغة
جزالة

سحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qur'an's inimitability is only about its content (e.g., prophecies, scientific facts), but the author highlights the unique verbal artistry as a distinct proof.
Correction: The passage shows that the very wording and style of the Qur'an are miraculous, as admitted by skilled Arab poets and orators.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the eloquent Arabs say about the Qur'an's wording?
A) It is poetry.
B) It is magic that is transmitted.
C) It is human speech.
D) It is inferior to other scriptures.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's linguistic inimitability is proven by its unmatched eloquence, as admitted even by the most skilled Arab poets and orators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the Qur'an, do you ever feel overwhelmed by its beauty as the Arabs did, or has familiarity made you indifferent? What would it take for you to experience that original awe? Choose a short surah today, read it slowly, and ask yourself: can a human really have produced this? Let that question unsettle you.
?
The eloquent Arabs admitted their inability to produce anything like the Qur'an and called it magic. But you have the advantage of understanding its meaning. Are you truly convinced that no human speech compares to it? If so, how should that transform the way you speak, write, and value your own words? Will you let the Qur'an's eloquence humble your tongue and refine your character?

The Inimitability of the Qur'an in Meaning and Fulfilled Prophecies
Author argues for the Qur'an's i'jāz in its profound wisdom and accurate prophecies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the miraculous nature of the Qur'an's content, including its profound wisdom, accurate historical accounts, and fulfilled prophecies, all of which point to its divine origin.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's content—its wisdom, history, and prophecies—is miraculously beyond human capacity.

In this passage, the author continues the proof of the Qur'an's inimitability, now focusing on its meaning rather than its wording. He argues that the Qur'an contains wondrous wisdoms that no philosopher could produce, especially from an unlettered prophet. Moreover, its accurate stories of past prophets and fulfilled prophecies are clear miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overall sense. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents two main arguments: the wisdom content and the prophecies.
Pay special attention to the description of the Prophet's background.
2
Analyze the argument about wisdom
Identify the claim: the Qur'an contains wondrous wisdoms and novel meanings that the philosophers and ancients could not produce. The author stresses the Prophet's illiteracy and lack of education to highlight the miraculous nature.
The key phrase is 'بدائع المعاني' (novel meanings).
3
Examine the evidence from stories
The Qur'an includes accurate stories of past prophets, which the Prophet could not have known by natural means. This is presented as a manifest miracle.
The author uses rhetorical questions to emphasize the impossibility of human knowledge.
4
Consider the fulfilled prophecies
The passage mentions specific prophecies: the victory of the religion and the defeat of the Romans, which were fulfilled. These serve as tangible proof of divine origin.
The phrase 'رأيناه طابق الحال' indicates the fulfillment was observed.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
The author concludes that these aspects together prove the Qur'an's inimitability in meaning. Consider how this complements the earlier argument about wording.
Think about why the author emphasizes the Prophet's unlettered state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من حيث المعنى فما اشتمل عليه القرآن من غرائب الحكم وبدائع المعاني التي عجزت الحكماء والأوائل عن الإتيان بمثلها نوع آخر من الإعجاز، خصوصا ممن نشأ يتيما بين الأميين من العرب، ولم يقرأ كتاباً ولم يدرس علما، ولا تلقف من أستاذ، ولا تعلم من معلم، فلولا أنه وحي محض يوحى عليه وتنزيل نزل إليه، وإلا فمن أين لطبع مجرد يجود بمثله، وأيضا فما يشتمل عليه القرآن من قصص الأنبياء والمرسلين كيف جرت دعوتهم؟ ومن أين أجاب كلمتهم؟ ومن نكل عنهم إلى أي حال آل أمرهم فمن لم يسمع ذلك من أحد ولا خط كتابا بيمينه ولا طالع كتب الأنبياء والتواريخ والأخبار معجز ظاهر، ودليل على أن الحق باهر، وهذا في قصة الماضين فما قولك في الأخبار عن الغيوب مما سيكون في ثاني الحال مما رأيناه طابق الحال وصدق المقال إذا أخبر أنه يظهره على الدين كله، وقد أظهر وأخبر أنه غلبت الروم وقد تحقق، وغير ذلك من الأخبار.
And as for with respect to meaning, what the Qur'an contains of wondrous wisdoms and novel meanings at which the philosophers and ancients were incapable of bringing the like of them is another type of inimitability, especially from one who grew up an orphan among the illiterate Arabs, and did not read a book nor study knowledge, nor did he learn from a teacher, nor did he take from a professor. So if it were not pure revelation revealed upon him and a sending down sent down to him, then how could a bare nature have the capacity to produce something like it? And also what the Qur'an contains of the stories of the prophets and messengers, how their call went, from where did they answer his word, and those who turned away from them, to what state did their affair end? So whoever did not hear that from anyone and did not write a book with his right hand and did not peruse the books of the prophets, histories, and reports, it is a manifest miracle and a proof that the truth is overwhelming. This is concerning the people of the past. So what do you say about the reports of the unseen of what will happen in the future, which we have seen matched the event and confirmed the speech, when He informed that He would make it prevail over the entire religion, and it prevailed, and He informed that the Romans would be defeated, and it came true, and other such reports.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the proof of the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jāz), now focusing on its content (ma'nā). The author makes two main points: (1) The Qur'an contains wondrous wisdoms (gharā'ib al-ḥikam) and novel meanings (badā'i' al-ma'ānī) that the philosophers and ancients (al-ḥukamā' wa al-awā'il) could not produce. This is especially remarkable because the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) grew up an orphan among the illiterate Arabs (al-ummiyyīn), never read a book, never studied, never took from a teacher. So if it were not pure revelation (waḥy maḥḍ), how could an untutored nature produce such a thing? (2) The Qur'an also contains accurate reports of past prophets and their stories, which the Prophet could not have known except by revelation. Moreover, it contains prophecies of future events (al-akhbār 'an al-ghuyūb) that came true, such as the victory of the religion and the defeat of the Romans. These fulfilled prophecies are a manifest miracle (mu'jiz ẓāhir) and a proof that the truth is overwhelming (al-ḥaqq bāhir). The author then concludes this section with 'wa ghayru dhālika min al-akhbār' (and other such reports).

Key Terms
إعجاز
حكم
بدائع المعاني
وحي
قصص الأنبياء
أخبار الغيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Muhammad acquired his knowledge from teachers or books.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the Prophet grew up illiterate among illiterate Arabs, never read a book, never studied, so the Qur'an's wisdom can only be from divine revelation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as evidence of the Qur'an's inimitability in meaning?
A) Wondrous wisdoms and novel meanings.
B) Accurate stories of past prophets.
C) The linguistic eloquence of its verses.
D) Fulfilled prophecies of future events.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's inimitability in meaning is proven by its profound wisdom, accurate historical reports, and fulfilled prophecies, all of which the unlettered Prophet could not have produced without divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the Qur'an contains prophecies that came true exactly. Are you living today as if the Qur'an's warnings about the Day of Judgment will also come true with the same certainty? What concrete change can you make in your daily routine to reflect that belief?
?
The Prophet was unlettered, yet the Qur'an's wisdom surpasses all philosophers. When you open the Qur'an, do you approach it as the words of the All-Knowing God, or do you read it as a mere historical text? Challenge yourself: before your next reading, pause and say, 'This is from God, and I must obey.'
?
This lesson shows that the Qur'an's miracles are not just in its language but in its profound meaning and accurate news. Have you ever reflected on a verse that gives you a specific command and asked yourself, 'Am I trivializing this by reading it without acting?' Pick one verse you often recite and commit to acting on it this week.

Inimitability in Legal Rulings and Comprehensive Nature
Author argues that the Qur'an's comprehensive laws and superiority over previous scriptures prove i'jāz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an's comprehensive legal system and its superiority over previous scriptures serve as a proof of its inimitability (i'jāz).

Core Idea
The Qur'an's laws are a miracle that perfects both worldly life and the afterlife.

In this final proof of the Qur'an's inimitability, the author shifts from linguistic miracle to legal comprehensiveness. He argues that the rulings on worship, transactions, and governance combine to reform worldly life and secure salvation, built on universal principles. Then he compares the Qur'an to previous scriptures, showing its superiority in clarity despite conciseness. This passage solidifies the Qur'an as both inimitable (mu'jiz) in form and delightful (mu'jib) in meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage without attempting to analyze. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the list of rulings: عبادات، معاملات، حلال، حرام، حدود، قصاص، ديات، سياسات.
2
Identify the miraculous aspects of the rulings
The author states that these rulings combine reforming livelihood (إصلاح المعاش) and salvation (نجاة المعاد) on universal principles (قوانين كلية). Consider how that makes them a miracle.
3
Compare the Qur'an to previous scriptures
The author says that the Qur'an contains all that is in the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms, but with clearer expression and indication despite conciseness. Note the contrast.
4
Reflect on the summary conclusion
The passage concludes that the Qur'an is mu'jiz in form and mu'jib in meaning, and is a proof of Allah and the Prophet. Understand the distinction between mu'jiz and mu'jib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك ما اشتمل عليه القرآن من أنواع العبادات والمعاملات من أحكام الحلال والحرام والحدود والقصاص والديات وأحكام السياسات إعجاز ظاهر؛ إذ جمع فيها بين إصلاح المعاش ونجاة المعاد، وبناها على قوانين كلية تجمع شمل العامة، وتناظر عقل الخاصة، ومن قرأ الكتب المنزلة من التوراة والإنجيل والزبور تبين له الفرق الظاهر بينها وبين القرآن؛ إذ كل ما اشتملت عليه من الحقائق العقلية والأحكام الشرعية على بسطها اشتمل عليه القرآن بأوضح عبارة وأوضح إشارة على اختصاصه، ومن أراد أن يتخطى عما أقررناه إلى مفردات الحروف وكيفيات تركيبها وما فيها من الحكم والمعاني قضى منها العجب، وبالجملة القرآن بصورة ألفاظه وعباراته معجز، وبمعنى حقائقه وعجائبه معجب، وهو دليل ظاهر على حقيقة ذاته وصدق المتحدي بآياته.
And likewise, what the Qur'an contains of types of acts of worship and transactions, of rulings of the lawful and unlawful, prescribed penalties, retaliation, blood money, and rulings of governance, is a manifest miracle; for it combines in them the reform of livelihood and the salvation of the hereafter, and built them upon universal principles that unite the common people and are consistent with the intellect of the elite. And whoever reads the revealed scriptures of the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms, the manifest difference between them and the Qur'an becomes clear to him; for everything they contain of intellectual truths and legal rulings in their elaboration, the Qur'an contains with the clearest expression and the clearest indication, despite its conciseness. And whoever wishes to go beyond what we have established to the individual letters and the modalities of their composition and what is in them of wisdoms and meanings, he would be filled with wonder. In summary, the Qur'an in the form of its words and expressions is inimitable (mu'jiz), and in the meaning of its truths and wonders it is delightful (mu'jib), and it is a manifest proof of the reality of His Essence and the truthfulness of the one who challenges with its verses.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is the final proof of the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jāz), focusing on its legal and comprehensive nature. The author argues that the Qur'an's rulings on acts of worship (ʿibādāt), transactions (muʿāmalāt), lawful and unlawful (ḥalāl wa ḥarām), prescribed penalties (ḥudūd), retaliation (qiṣāṣ), blood money (diyāt), and governance (siyāsāt) constitute a manifest miracle (mu'jiz ẓāhir). These laws combine reform of livelihood (iṣlāḥ al-maʿāsh) and salvation in the hereafter (najāt al-maʿād), built upon universal principles (qawānīn kulliyya) that appeal to both the common people (al-ʿāmma) and the elite (al-khāṣṣa). The author then compares the Qur'an to previous scriptures (Torah, Gospel, Psalms): the Qur'an contains everything they do, but with the clearest expression (awḍaḥ ʿibāra) and clearest indication (awḍaḥ ishāra), despite its conciseness (ikhtiṣāṣ). He then mentions that even the individual letters and their composition contain wisdoms that amaze. Finally, he summarizes: the Qur'an is inimitable in its verbal form (ṣūrat alfāẓihi wa ʿibārātihi) and delightful in its meanings (maʿnā ḥaqā'iqihi wa ʿajā'ibihi). It is a manifest proof (dalīl ẓāhir) of God's Essence (dhāt) and the truthfulness of the Prophet who challenges (al-mutaḥaddī) with its verses.

سياسات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an's inimitability is only linguistic.
Correction: The author shows that it also lies in its comprehensive legal system covering worship, transactions, penalties, governance, and its superiority over previous scriptures.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the Qur'an and previous scriptures?
A) The Qur'an contradicts them entirely.
B) The Qur'an contains all their truths with clearer expression.
C) The Qur'an is inferior in legal rulings.
D) The Qur'an is a copy of the Torah.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's comprehensive laws and superiority over previous scriptures provide a manifest proof of its inimitability and the truthfulness of the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Qur'an’s laws are a miracle that reforms both your worldly life and your afterlife, why do you still seek guidance from modern ideologies or personal whims in areas where the Qur'an has clearly spoken? What would it look like for you to truly submit to these laws as the ultimate proof of prophethood?
?
The author says the Qur'an surpasses all previous scriptures in clarity and conciseness. Do you live with the deep conviction that the Qur'an is sufficient for every aspect of your life? If not, what specific area are you still hesitant to trust the Qur'an fully? Repent and realign today.
?
When you read the legal verses of the Qur'an — on transactions, penalties, or governance — do you experience the same awe and wonder as when you read its linguistic miracles? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of i'jāz? Ask Allah to open your heart to the comprehensive miracle of His Book.

The Miracle of Quranic Stories of Prophets
Shahrastani presents the Quran's stories of past prophets as a manifest miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's accurate narration of past prophetic stories serves as a manifest miracle, especially given the Prophet's illiteracy.

Core Idea
The Quran's accurate accounts of past prophets prove it is from God.

The Quran contains detailed stories of prophets and their communities. Shahrastani argues that these narratives are a clear miracle because the Prophet Muhammad was unlettered and had no access to previous scriptures. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the passage once to get an overview. Notice the three questions posed about the prophets.
The passage begins with 'فما يشتمل عليه القرآن من قصص الأنبياء...'
2
Identify the three questions
The passage asks: how did the prophets' call proceed? From where was their word answered? To what state did those who turned away come?
These questions set up the miraculous nature of the Quran's narratives.
3
Reflect on the Prophet's illiteracy
Consider why the fact that Muhammad (peace be upon him) could not read or write, and had no access to prior scriptures, makes the Quran's detailed stories a miracle.
Shahrastani points to the Prophet's lack of prior knowledge as key evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما يشتمل عليه القرآن من قصص الأنبياء والمرسلين كيف جرت دعوتهم؟ ومن أين أجيب كلمتهم؟ ومن نكل عنهم إلى أي حال آل أمرهم؟ فمن لم يسمع ذلك من أحد ولا خط كتابا بيمينه ولا طالع كتب الأنبياء والتواريخ والأخبار معجز ظاهر، ودليل على أن الحق باهر. وهذا في قصة الماضين. فما قولك في الأخبار عن الغيوب مما سيكون في ثاني الحال مما رأيناه طابق الحال وصدق المقال إذا أخبر أنه يظهره على الدين كله، وقد أظهرذلك. وأخبر أنه غلبت الروم وقد تحقق، وغير ذلك من الأخبار. وكذلك ما اشتمل عليه القرآن من أنواع العبادات والمعاملات من أحكام الحلال والحرام والحدود والقصاص والديات وأحكام السياسات إعجاز ظاهر؛ إذ جمع فيها بين إصلاح المعاش ونجاة المعاد، وبناها على قوانين كلية تجمع شمل العامة، وتناظر عقل الخاصة. ومن قرأ الكتب المنزلة من التوراة والإنجيل والزبور تبين له الفرق الظاهر بينها وبين القرآن؛ إذ كل ما اشتملت عليه من الحقائق العقلية والأحكام الشرعية على بسطها اشتمل عليه القرآن بأوضح عبارة وأوضح إشارة على اختصاصه. ومن أراد أن يتخطى عما أقررناه إلى مفردات الحروف وكيفيات تركيبها وما فيها من الحكم والمعاني قضى منها العجب. وبالجملة القرآن بصورة ألفاظه وعباراته معجز، وبمعنى حقائقه وعجائبه معجب، وهو دليل ظاهر على حقيقة ذاته وصدق المتحدي بآياته.
What the Quran contains of the stories of the prophets and messengers—how did their call proceed? And from where was their word answered? And those who turned away—to what state did their affair end? Whoever has not heard that from anyone, nor written a book with his right hand, nor perused the books of the prophets, histories, and reports—[this is] a manifest miracle, and proof that the truth is overwhelming. This concerns the stories of the past. What do you say about the reports of the unseen of what will happen in the second state, which we have seen match the state and confirm the statement—when He informed that He would make it prevail over the entire religion, and He has made it prevail? And He informed that the Romans would be victorious, and it has been realized, and other such reports. Likewise, what the Quran contains of the types of acts of worship and transactions—judgments of the lawful and unlawful, prescribed punishments, retribution, blood-money, and rulings of governance—[this is] a manifest miracle, for when it combined therein between the reform of livelihood and the salvation of the afterlife, and built them upon universal laws that unite the common people and correspond to the intellect of the elite. Whoever reads the revealed books—the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms—the manifest difference between them and the Quran becomes clear to him, for everything they contain of rational truths and legal rulings in their elaboration, the Quran encompasses with the clearest expression and the clearest indication, in its uniqueness. And whoever wishes to go beyond what we have established to the individual letters and the modalities of their composition and what they contain of wisdoms and meanings would be amazed by them. In summary, the Quran is miraculous in the form of its words and expressions, and wondrous in the meaning of its truths and marvels, and it is a manifest proof of the reality of His essence and the truthfulness of the challenger through His verses.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is an expository argument for the miraculous nature (i'jaz) of the Quran. The author (Shahrastani) lists several aspects. First, the Quran's narratives of past prophets and their communities—how they called, how they were answered, and the fate of rejecters—serve as a manifest miracle, especially as Muhammad (peace be upon him) was unlettered and had no prior knowledge of these stories. Second, the Quran's prophecies about future events, such as the triumph of the Romans and the predominance of Islam, which were later fulfilled, are clear signs. Third, the Quran's comprehensive legislation covering worship, transactions, criminal law, and governance combines worldly reform and otherworldly salvation through universal principles accessible to both common people and intellectuals. Fourth, a comparison with previous scriptures (Torah, Gospel, Psalms) shows the Quran's superiority in concise clarity while containing all rational truths and legal rulings. Fifth, even the individual letters and their compositional intricacies contain wonders. In conclusion, the Quran is miraculous in both form (words and expressions) and meaning (truths and wonders), serving as a clear proof of God's reality and the truth of His prophet's challenge.

Key Terms
قصص الأنبياء
مرسلين
معجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran's miracle is only in its linguistic style.
Correction: The Quran's accurate narration of past events also constitutes a miracle, especially given the Prophet's illiteracy.

Checkpoint
What does Shahrastani cite as evidence that the Quran's stories of prophets are a manifest miracle?
A) The stories are detailed and accurate.
B) The Prophet could not read or write and had not studied previous books.
C) The stories contain moral lessons.
D) The stories are unique to the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's accurate accounts of past prophets, despite the Prophet's illiteracy, are a manifest miracle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe the Quran's stories are true, but do you live as if the fate of those who rejected their prophets could be your own if you turn away? What is one specific way you have ignored a prophetic warning in your life, and what will you do today to change that?
?
The Prophet ﷺ was unlettered, yet he brought knowledge beyond his time. Do you approach the Quran with the same humility, or do you assume you can understand it without submitting to its authority? What is one verse you have read but not acted upon?
?
If the Quran's accuracy about past nations is a miracle, how much more weight does its promise of the Hereafter carry? Are you preparing for that meeting as urgently as if you were one of those nations being addressed? Name one worldly attachment that you need to sever today because it distracts you from eternal success.

Fulfilled Prophecies: A Miracle of the Quran
Shahrastani cites fulfilled prophecies as evidence of the Quran's miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's prophecies about future events, such as the victory of the Romans, serve as a clear miracle.

Core Idea
The Quran's accurate predictions of the future prove it is divine.

The Quran contains prophecies about events that later came true, such as the Roman victory. Shahrastani uses this as evidence of the Quran's miraculous nature. This lesson examines his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about prophecies
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two specific prophecies mentioned.
The passage starts with 'فما قولك في الأخبار عن الغيوب...'
2
Identify the two prophecies
The passage mentions that Islam would prevail over all religions and that the Romans would be victorious. Both came true.
These are historical events that occurred after the Quran's revelation.
3
Understand why fulfilled prophecy is miraculous
Reflect on the impossibility of a human knowing the future with such accuracy. This demonstrates divine knowledge.
Shahrastani uses these as clear proofs of the Quran's divine origin.

Second, the Quran's prophecies about future events, such as the triumph of the Romans and the predominance of Islam, which were later fulfilled, are clear signs. Third, the Quran's comprehensive legislation covering worship, transactions, criminal law, and governance combines worldly reform and otherworldly salvation through universal principles accessible to both common people and intellectuals. Fourth, a comparison with previous scriptures (Torah, Gospel, Psalms) shows the Quran's superiority in concise clarity while containing all rational truths and legal rulings. Fifth, even the individual letters and their compositional intricacies contain wonders. In conclusion, the Quran is miraculous in both form (words and expressions) and meaning (truths and wonders), serving as a clear proof of God's reality and the truth of His prophet's challenge.
