The Miracle of Quranic Law: Combining Worldly and Religious Benefits
Shahrastani presents the Quran's legal content as a manifest miracle for its balance and universal principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's comprehensive legal system, covering worship and transactions, is a manifest miracle.

Core Idea
The Quran's laws perfectly balance worldly and religious needs—a sign of divine origin.

The Quranic legal rulings are not merely divine commands; they are also practical and beneficial. Shahrastani argues that they are a miracle because they integrate worldly reform with afterlife salvation. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on legislation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the different categories of law mentioned.
The passage begins with 'وكذلك ما اشتمل عليه القرآن من أنواع العبادات...'
2
List the types of laws
The passage lists worship, transactions, halal/haram, hudud, qisas, diyat, and governance.
These cover all aspects of life.
3
Reflect on the dual purpose
Consider how these laws combine reform of livelihood and salvation of the afterlife, built on universal principles accessible to all.
This combination is beyond human capacity, hence miraculous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما يشتمل عليه القرآن من قصص الأنبياء والمرسلين كيف جرت دعوتهم؟ ومن أين أجيب كلمتهم؟ ومن نكل عنهم إلى أي حال آل أمرهم؟ فمن لم يسمع ذلك من أحد ولا خط كتابا بيمينه ولا طالع كتب الأنبياء والتواريخ والأخبار معجز ظاهر، ودليل على أن الحق باهر. وهذا في قصة الماضين. فما قولك في الأخبار عن الغيوب مما سيكون في ثاني الحال مما رأيناه طابق الحال وصدق المقال إذا أخبر أنه يظهره على الدين كله، وقد أظهرذلك. وأخبر أنه غلبت الروم وقد تحقق، وغير ذلك من الأخبار. وكذلك ما اشتمل عليه القرآن من أنواع العبادات والمعاملات من أحكام الحلال والحرام والحدود والقصاص والديات وأحكام السياسات إعجاز ظاهر؛ إذ جمع فيها بين إصلاح المعاش ونجاة المعاد، وبناها على قوانين كلية تجمع شمل العامة، وتناظر عقل الخاصة. ومن قرأ الكتب المنزلة من التوراة والإنجيل والزبور تبين له الفرق الظاهر بينها وبين القرآن؛ إذ كل ما اشتملت عليه من الحقائق العقلية والأحكام الشرعية على بسطها اشتمل عليه القرآن بأوضح عبارة وأوضح إشارة على اختصاصه. ومن أراد أن يتخطى عما أقررناه إلى مفردات الحروف وكيفيات تركيبها وما فيها من الحكم والمعاني قضى منها العجب. وبالجملة القرآن بصورة ألفاظه وعباراته معجز، وبمعنى حقائقه وعجائبه معجب، وهو دليل ظاهر على حقيقة ذاته وصدق المتحدي بآياته.
What the Quran contains of the stories of the prophets and messengers—how did their call proceed? And from where was their word answered? And those who turned away—to what state did their affair end? Whoever has not heard that from anyone, nor written a book with his right hand, nor perused the books of the prophets, histories, and reports—[this is] a manifest miracle, and proof that the truth is overwhelming. This concerns the stories of the past. What do you say about the reports of the unseen of what will happen in the second state, which we have seen match the state and confirm the statement—when He informed that He would make it prevail over the entire religion, and He has made it prevail? And He informed that the Romans would be victorious, and it has been realized, and other such reports. Likewise, what the Quran contains of the types of acts of worship and transactions—judgments of the lawful and unlawful, prescribed punishments, retribution, blood-money, and rulings of governance—[this is] a manifest miracle, for when it combined therein between the reform of livelihood and the salvation of the afterlife, and built them upon universal laws that unite the common people and correspond to the intellect of the elite. Whoever reads the revealed books—the Torah, the Gospel, and the Psalms—the manifest difference between them and the Quran becomes clear to him, for everything they contain of rational truths and legal rulings in their elaboration, the Quran encompasses with the clearest expression and the clearest indication, in its uniqueness. And whoever wishes to go beyond what we have established to the individual letters and the modalities of their composition and what they contain of wisdoms and meanings would be amazed by them. In summary, the Quran is miraculous in the form of its words and expressions, and wondrous in the meaning of its truths and marvels, and it is a manifest proof of the reality of His essence and the truthfulness of the challenger through His verses.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is an expository argument for the miraculous nature (i'jaz) of the Quran. The author (Shahrastani) lists several aspects. First, the Quran's narratives of past prophets and their communities—how they called, how they were answered, and the fate of rejecters—serve as a manifest miracle, especially as Muhammad (peace be upon him) was unlettered and had no prior knowledge of these stories. Second, the Quran's prophecies about future events, such as the triumph of the Romans and the predominance of Islam, which were later fulfilled, are clear signs. Third, the Quran's comprehensive legislation covering worship, transactions, criminal law, and governance combines worldly reform and otherworldly salvation through universal principles accessible to both common people and intellectuals. Fourth, a comparison with previous scriptures (Torah, Gospel, Psalms) shows the Quran's superiority in concise clarity while containing all rational truths and legal rulings. Fifth, even the individual letters and their compositional intricacies contain wonders. In conclusion, the Quran is miraculous in both form (words and expressions) and meaning (truths and wonders), serving as a clear proof of God's reality and the truth of His prophet's challenge.

The Quran's Superiority: Containing All Rational Truths and Laws
Shahrastani compares the Quran to previous scriptures and declares its superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran surpasses previous scriptures by encompassing their truths in clearer expressions.

Core Idea
The Quran compresses all rational truths and laws of earlier books into clearer expressions—a miracle.

Shahrastani challenges us to compare the Quran with the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms. He claims the Quran includes everything they contain but with greater clarity and conciseness. This lesson examines that comparative argument.

Guided Path

Read the passage comparing scriptures

2
Compare the Quran to previous books
Shahrastani says the Quran contains all rational truths and legal rulings from the Torah, Gospel, and Psalms.
But with the clearest expression and indication.
3
Understand the claim of uniqueness
Reflect on how the Quran's brevity and clarity, while covering the same content, is a miracle.
This demonstrates that the Quran is from the same divine source but superior in presentation.

The Miracle of Quranic Letters and Final Summary
Shahrastani discusses the letters of the Quran and summarizes the overall miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how even the individual letters of the Quran contain wonders, and the overall miraculous nature of the Quran in both form and meaning.

Core Idea
The Quran is miraculous in its words and meanings, proving God's reality and the Prophet's truth.

Shahrastani concludes his argument by pointing to the individual letters and composition of the Quran. He then summarizes the entire discussion, stating that the Quran is miraculous in form and meaning. This lesson captures that final assertion.

Guided Path

Understand the miracle of letters
Shahrastani says that studying the individual letters and their composition reveals amazing wisdoms and meanings.
This points to a micro-level miracle in the Quran's structure.
3
Grasp the final summary
The Quran is miraculous in form (words and expressions) and meaning (truths and marvels). It is a clear proof of God's essence and the Prophet's truthfulness.
This concludes the argument for the Quran's miraculous nature.

The Clarity Criterion for Miracles
Second objection raises definitional and epistemological issues with linguistic miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the linguistic miracle of the Quran, focusing on the requirement that a miracle must be undeniably evident to everyone.

Core Idea
A miracle must be as clear as a rock turning into water.

The opponents now press a deeper point: even if we grant that the Quran's inimitability lies in its linguistic qualities, those qualities are neither clearly defined nor agreed upon. Moreover, a true miracle should be unmistakably apparent to all—like a stone turning into water. This passage challenges the very epistemological status of the Quran's miraculous nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the two main prongs of the objection: the lack of definition and the internal disagreement among Muslims.
Notice the shift from asking about definitions to questioning the clarity of the miracle.
2
Analyze the criterion for a true miracle
The objectors state a miracle must be 'apparent to everyone without doubt.' Compare this with the disputed linguistic qualities.
This is the core of the objection—epistemic clarity.
3
Reflect on the comparison with physical miracles
The objectors contrast the Quran with clear sensory miracles like the sea parting or a rock turning to water. Why is this comparison powerful?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أنكم قلتم: وجه إعجاز القرآن فصاحته وجزالته ونظمه وبلاغته، فما حدود هذه المعاني؟ أولا حتى نبين حقائقها فنتكلم عليها، أهي بإفرادها معجزة أم بمجموعها؟ وقد رأينا كم اختلفتم في أن القرآن معجزة من جهة صرف الدواعي، أم من جهة ما اشتمل عليه من بديع النظم والفصاحة، ومن قال بالقسم الثاني اختلف في أن الفصاحة بديعة أم الجزالة أم النظم، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أن المعجز يجب أن يكون ظاهراً لكل من هو في حقه معجز، ظهورا لا يستراب فيه البتة، ومن قال منكم: إنه معجز من حيث الفصاحة فقط جوز أن يكون في كلام العرب مثله من حيث النظم والجزالة ومن قال بالثاني فقد جوز المماثلة في الأول فلم يظهر ظهور انقلاب الجماد حيوانًا، والبحر يبسا، والحجر ماءً.
And the second: You have said: The aspect of the Quran's miraculousness is its eloquence (fasahah), its robustness (jazalah), its structure (nazm), and its rhetorical beauty (balaghah). So what are the limits of these meanings? First, so that we may clarify their realities and speak on them. Are they individually miraculous or collectively? And we have seen how much you have disagreed: whether the Quran is miraculous from the aspect of diverting inclinations (ṣarf al-dawāʿī), or from the aspect of what it contains of innovative structure and eloquence. And whoever said the second division disagreed: is it the eloquence that is innovative, or the robustness, or the structure? And it is known without doubt that the miracle must be apparent to everyone for whom it is a miracle, an appearance in which there is no doubt at all. And whoever among you said that it is miraculous only from the aspect of eloquence has allowed that there could be in the speech of the Arabs something like it in terms of structure and robustness. And whoever said the second has allowed similarity in the first. Thus it does not appear like the transformation of an inanimate object into an animal, the sea into dry land, or stone into water.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is the second objection, targeting the lack of precise definition and agreement among Muslims about the nature of the Quran's linguistic miracle. The opponents note that scholars (the mutakallimūn) claim the Quran's miracle lies in its eloquence (fasahah), robustness (jazalah), structure (nazm), and rhetorical beauty (balaghah). But they ask: What are the precise definitions of these terms? Are each individually miraculous or only collectively? Moreover, they point out internal disagreement: Some say the miracle is the 'diverting of inclinations' (ṣarf al-dawāʿī), meaning God prevents people from imitating it; others say it is the inherent literary excellence. Even among the latter, there is disagreement whether it is eloquence, robustness, or structure. The opponents then present a key criterion: a miracle must be absolutely clear to everyone for whom it is a proof, with no room for doubt. But if the Quran's miracle is only a matter of eloquence, then it is possible that some Arab speech matches it in structure and robustness, diminishing its uniqueness. If the miracle is only in one specific quality, the other qualities might be imitable. Consequently, the Quran's miraculousness is not as clear and undeniable as physical miracles like an inanimate object turning into an animal, the sea turning to dry land, or stone turning to water—which are unambiguous sensory events. Thus they argue that the linguistic miracle lacks the required clarity and consensus.

Key Terms
فصاحة
جزالة
نظم
بلاغة
صرف الدواعي
بديع النظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second objection denies that the Quran is a miracle at all.
Correction: The objection does not deny the miracle; it questions whether the linguistic miracle is as epistemically clear as sensory miracles, given the lack of consensus and definition.

Checkpoint
What criterion do the objectors present that a true miracle must satisfy?
A) It must be eloquent.
B) It must be accepted by all scholars.
C) It must be clearly apparent to everyone without doubt.
D) It must be impossible to imitate.

Key Takeaway
The second objection challenges the Quran's linguistic miracle by demanding clear definitions and pointing to its lack of universal clarity compared to physical miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you truly feel that its words could not have been produced by any human, or does your heart entertain doubts like those raised in this objection? What would it take for you to be as certain of its miraculousness as if you saw a stone turn into water?
?
If a non-Muslim asked you today to prove that the Quran is a miracle, could you give a clear and undeniable answer? Where does your confidence come from—rational arguments or personal experience? This lesson forces you to confront whether you have truly internalized the Quran's miraculous nature.
?
The objector demands that a miracle be 'apparent without doubt to everyone.' Is your belief in the Quran's miraculousness so firm that you would stake your life on it—or does a small hesitation remain? If there is hesitation, are you willing to honestly search for the cause, rather than simply citing inherited arguments?

Miracles
beginner
5 min
The Eternal and Acquired Cannot Be Miracles
Opening negations and first modality of Qur'anic miracle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal (God) cannot be a miracle and why human acquisition of the Qur'an is not the miracle itself.

Core Idea
Miracles are created acts, not the Creator or human acquisition.

This opening statement establishes two crucial negations: the Eternal (God) cannot be a miracle because He is the creator of miracles, and what is acquired by the servant (like the act of recitation) cannot be the miracle itself because it is within human power. The author then introduces that the Qur'an has multiple miraculous aspects, beginning with the first possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
2
Identify the two negations
Find where the author says 'the eternal is impossible to be a miracle' and 'what is acquired by the servant is likewise'. Understand why each cannot be a miracle.
3
Notice the first modality
The author begins the enumeration with the first aspect: God creates the letters directly in the reciter's tongue. Observe that the miracle is in the arrangement, not the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق أما السؤال الأول فنقول : القديم يستحيل أن يكون معجزة والمكتسب للعبد كذلك، وللقرآن وجوه من المعجزات ولكل وجه وجوه من التقديرات، فيجوز أن يقال : إن التلاوة من حيث إنها تلاوة معجزة وتقدير الإعجاز فيها من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يخلق الله تعالى هذه الحروف المركبة وتلك الكلمات المنظومة في لسان التالي من غير أن يكون قادرًا عليها ومحركا لسانه بقدرته واستطاعته، فعل الله تعالى، ويظهر إعجازه في نظمه المخصوص، ويجوز أن يخلق الله في نفس النبي كلاما منظوما فيترجم عنه بلسانه، ويكون تحريك اللسان مقدوراً له لكن الكلام المعجز ما اشتمل عليه الضمير ونعت في الصدور، كما قال تعالى: ﴿ولا تحرك به لسانك لتعجل به إن علينا جمعه وقرآنه﴾ أي: في صدرك وقلبك، فإذا قرأناه أي جمعناه فاتبع قرآنه. ويجوز أن يخلق الله ذلك الكلام في قلب الملك أو في لسانه فيلقيه وحيًا إلى قلب النبي ويعبر عنه النبي بلسانه، كما قال تعالى: ﴿إنه لقول رسول كريم﴾ أي تنزيلاً ووحياً، وكما قال: ﴿علمه شديد القوى﴾. ويجوز أن يكون قد خلق الله هذه العبارات المنظومة بعينها في اللوح المحفوظ فيقرأ جبريل عليه السلام منه، ويقرأها على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيسمع النبي منه كما يسمع الواحد منا ويكون المعجز هو الكلام المنظوم، وجبريل مظهر والنبي مظهر، كما أن واحدًا منا يقرأه ويكون مظهرًا، لكن إظهار جبريل كإظهارنا ليدل على صدق النبي عليه السلام، وهذا كما خلق الله تعالى الناقة في الصخرة ثم أظهرها منها عند دعوة صالح عليه السلام فيكون إظهار المعجزة مقرونا بالدعوى والتحدي، لا إظهار المعجز كخلق المعجز في الدلالة على الصدق. وينبغي أن ننبه هاهنا على هذه الدقيقة وهي أنا إذا روينا شعر الشاعر نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على التلفظ بذلك الشعر، ولا نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على نظم ذلك، بل ربما يكون الراوي عديم الطبع في إنشاء الشعر عن نظم مخصوص، فما المقدور منه وما غير المقدور؟ فنقول : إذا قرأنا شعرا من كتاب أو لسان وحفظناه ارتسم الخيال في القلب والنفس بذلك الشعر وتمكن من عبر بلسانه عنه، فيكون المسموع أو المحفوظ من حيث إنه سمعناه ثم نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على التلفظ به.
The people of truth said: As for the first question, we say: The eternal is impossible to be a miracle, and what is acquired by the servant is likewise. The Qur'an has aspects of miracles, and for each aspect there are aspects of estimations. So it is permissible to say: The recitation, insofar as it is a miraculous recitation, and the estimation of its miraculousness is from two aspects: One of them is that God Most High creates these composite letters and those arranged words in the tongue of the reciter, without him being capable of them or moving his tongue with his power and capacity—an act of God Most High—and He manifests His miracle in its particular arrangement. And it is permissible that God creates in the soul of the prophet arranged speech, and he translates it with his tongue, and the movement of the tongue is within his power, but the miraculous speech is what the conscience contains and what is described in the breasts, as God Most High said: 'Do not move your tongue with it to hasten it; indeed upon Us is its collection and its recitation' [Al-Qiyamah: 16-17], meaning: in your chest and heart. So when We have recited it, i.e., collected it, then follow its recitation [Al-Qiyamah: 18]. And it is permissible that God creates that speech in the heart of the angel or on his tongue, and he casts it as revelation into the heart of the prophet, and the prophet expresses it with his tongue, as God Most High said: 'Indeed it is the word of a noble messenger' [At-Takwir: 19], i.e., a sending down and revelation, and as He said: 'One severe in strength taught him' [An-Najm: 5]. And it is permissible that God has created these very arranged expressions in the Preserved Tablet, so Gabriel, peace be upon him, reads from it, and he reads it to the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, and the Prophet hears from him just as one of us hears, and the miracle is the arranged speech, and Gabriel is a manifestation and the Prophet is a manifestation, just as one of us reads it and is a manifestation. But the manifestation of Gabriel is like our manifestation to indicate the truthfulness of the Prophet, peace be upon him. And this is like when God Most High created the she-camel in the rock and then brought it forth from it at the call of Salih, peace be upon him. So the manifestation of the miracle is coupled with the claim and the challenge, not the manifestation of the miracle like the creation of the miracle in indicating truthfulness. And we should draw attention here to this subtle point: that when we recite the poetry of a poet, we feel within ourselves an ability to utter that poetry, but we do not feel within ourselves an ability to compose it. Indeed, perhaps the narrator is naturally incapable of creating poetry with a particular arrangement. So what part of it is within power and what part is not? We say: When we read poetry from a book or a tongue and memorize it, an image of that poetry is imprinted in the heart and soul, and one is able to express it with his tongue. So what is heard or memorized, insofar as we have heard it, then we feel within ourselves an ability to utter it.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument by the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) responding to a question about the miraculous nature of the Qur'an. The author begins by stating that the eternal (al-qadim) cannot be a miracle, nor can something acquired by the servant (al-muktasab) be a miracle. The Qur'an has multiple aspects of miraculousness, each with various estimations. The discussion then enumerates the possible ways in which the recitation of the Qur'an is considered a miracle, presenting them as two main aspects (but listing four).

The first aspect: God directly creates the letters and words in the tongue of the reciter without the reciter's ability or movement—this is God's act, and the miracle appears in the specific arrangement (al-nazm al-makhsus).

Second aspect: God creates arranged speech in the soul of the prophet, who then translates it with his tongue. The prophet's tongue movement is within his power, but the miraculous speech is what the conscience contains, as indicated by the verse 'Do not move your tongue...' (Q. 75:16-18).

Third aspect: God creates that speech in the heart of the angel (Gabriel) or on his tongue, who then reveals it to the prophet's heart, and the prophet expresses it—supported by verses like 'It is the word of a noble messenger' (Q. 81:19) and 'One severe in strength taught him' (Q. 53:5).

Fourth aspect: God created the exact arranged expressions in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz). Gabriel reads from it and recites to the prophet, who hears it directly. The miracle is the arranged speech itself; both Gabriel and the prophet are manifestations (mazhir) of it. This is analogous to God creating a she-camel in the rock and then bringing it forth as a miracle for Prophet Salih. The manifestation (izhar) of the miracle is coupled with the claim and challenge, not the creation of the miracle itself.

Finally, the author adds a subtle point (al-daqiqa) about poetry: when we recite someone else's poetry, we feel capable of uttering it but not of composing it. The distinction is that what we have heard and memorized becomes imprinted in our hearts, enabling us to repeat it. This illustrates the difference between the miraculous composition and the act of recitation.

Key Terms
قديم
معجزة
مكتسب
تلاوة
نظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the Qur'an is recited by humans, the recitation itself is the miracle.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the miracle is the divine composition (nazm), not the human act of recitation, which is created by God directly in this modality.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the eternal (God) be a miracle according to the passage?
A) Because God is not subject to time
B) Because God is the creator of miracles, not a created miracle
C) Because miracles must be temporary
D) Because the Qur'an is eternal

Key Takeaway
The essence of the Qur'an's miracle is its divine arrangement, not the human act of recitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you ever feel a sense of pride in your own voice or skill? How does knowing that the miracle is entirely God's arrangement—not your performance—reshape that feeling? What would it take to let go of pride and stand in pure awe before His words?
?
If the divine arrangement of the Qur'an is the true miracle, ask yourself: have you ever treated the Qur'an as a human achievement or a cultural artifact? How might your daily engagement with it change if you truly believed every letter is a direct, miraculous act of God?
?
The passage denies that anything eternal can be a miracle. Have you ever expected God to 'prove' Himself through signs that fit your own standards? In what ways might you be demanding that the Eternal conform to your finite expectations? Will you repent from that and submit to His majesty?

Miracles
intermediate
7 min
Three Modalities: Prophet's Soul, Angel's Heart, Preserved Tablet
Three additional modalities and the analogy of Salih's she-camel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second, third, and fourth possible ways the Qur'an is a miracle according to the People of Truth.

Core Idea
The Qur'anic miracle can be through speech in the prophet's soul, angel's heart, or the Preserved Tablet.

After presenting the first modality where God creates the letters directly in the reciter's tongue, the author now covers three more possibilities: speech created in the prophet's soul, in the angel's heart, and pre-existing in the Preserved Tablet. Each emphasizes that the miraculous arrangement is from God, while the reciter or messenger is just a manifestation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully to see the flow of the three modalities and the concluding analogy.
2
Identify each modality
Find the three additional modalities: (2) speech in the prophet's soul, (3) speech in the angel's heart or tongue, (4) speech in the Preserved Tablet. Note how each attributes the miracle to divine creation.
3
Understand the role of manifestation
The author introduces the concept of 'mazhar' (manifestation) for Gabriel and the Prophet, analogizing to the she-camel of Salih. Understand that the miracle is the speech itself, not the one who recites it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق أما السؤال الأول فنقول : القديم يستحيل أن يكون معجزة والمكتسب للعبد كذلك، وللقرآن وجوه من المعجزات ولكل وجه وجوه من التقديرات، فيجوز أن يقال : إن التلاوة من حيث إنها تلاوة معجزة وتقدير الإعجاز فيها من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يخلق الله تعالى هذه الحروف المركبة وتلك الكلمات المنظومة في لسان التالي من غير أن يكون قادرًا عليها ومحركا لسانه بقدرته واستطاعته، فعل الله تعالى، ويظهر إعجازه في نظمه المخصوص، ويجوز أن يخلق الله في نفس النبي كلاما منظوما فيترجم عنه بلسانه، ويكون تحريك اللسان مقدوراً له لكن الكلام المعجز ما اشتمل عليه الضمير ونعت في الصدور، كما قال تعالى: ﴿ولا تحرك به لسانك لتعجل به إن علينا جمعه وقرآنه﴾ أي: في صدرك وقلبك، فإذا قرأناه أي جمعناه فاتبع قرآنه. ويجوز أن يخلق الله ذلك الكلام في قلب الملك أو في لسانه فيلقيه وحيًا إلى قلب النبي ويعبر عنه النبي بلسانه، كما قال تعالى: ﴿إنه لقول رسول كريم﴾ أي تنزيلاً ووحياً، وكما قال: ﴿علمه شديد القوى﴾. ويجوز أن يكون قد خلق الله هذه العبارات المنظومة بعينها في اللوح المحفوظ فيقرأ جبريل عليه السلام منه، ويقرأها على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيسمع النبي منه كما يسمع الواحد منا ويكون المعجز هو الكلام المنظوم، وجبريل مظهر والنبي مظهر، كما أن واحدًا منا يقرأه ويكون مظهرًا، لكن إظهار جبريل كإظهارنا ليدل على صدق النبي عليه السلام، وهذا كما خلق الله تعالى الناقة في الصخرة ثم أظهرها منها عند دعوة صالح عليه السلام فيكون إظهار المعجزة مقرونا بالدعوى والتحدي، لا إظهار المعجز كخلق المعجز في الدلالة على الصدق. وينبغي أن ننبه هاهنا على هذه الدقيقة وهي أنا إذا روينا شعر الشاعر نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على التلفظ بذلك الشعر، ولا نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على نظم ذلك، بل ربما يكون الراوي عديم الطبع في إنشاء الشعر عن نظم مخصوص، فما المقدور منه وما غير المقدور؟ فنقول : إذا قرأنا شعرا من كتاب أو لسان وحفظناه ارتسم الخيال في القلب والنفس بذلك الشعر وتمكن من عبر بلسانه عنه، فيكون المسموع أو المحفوظ من حيث إنه سمعناه ثم نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على التلفظ به.
The people of truth said: As for the first question, we say: The eternal is impossible to be a miracle, and what is acquired by the servant is likewise. But the manifestation of Gabriel is like our manifestation to indicate the truthfulness of the Prophet, peace be upon him. Commentary
This passage is a theological argument by the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) responding to a question about the miraculous nature of the Qur'an. The author begins by stating that the eternal (al-qadim) cannot be a miracle, nor can something acquired by the servant (al-muktasab) be a miracle. The discussion then enumerates the possible ways in which the recitation of the Qur'an is considered a miracle, presenting them as two main aspects (but listing four).

The first aspect: God directly creates the letters and words in the tongue of the reciter without the reciter's ability or movement—this is God's act, and the miracle appears in the specific arrangement (al-nazm al-makhsus).

Second aspect: God creates arranged speech in the soul of the prophet, who then translates it with his tongue. The prophet's tongue movement is within his power, but the miraculous speech is what the conscience contains, as indicated by the verse 'Do not move your tongue...' (Q. 75:16-18).

Third aspect: God creates that speech in the heart of the angel (Gabriel) or on his tongue, who then reveals it to the prophet's heart, and the prophet expresses it—supported by verses like 'It is the word of a noble messenger' (Q. 81:19) and 'One severe in strength taught him' (Q. 53:5).

Fourth aspect: God created the exact arranged expressions in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz). Gabriel reads from it and recites to the prophet, who hears it directly. The miracle is the arranged speech itself; both Gabriel and the prophet are manifestations (mazhir) of it. This is analogous to God creating a she-camel in the rock and then bringing it forth as a miracle for Prophet Salih. The manifestation (izhar) of the miracle is coupled with the claim and challenge, not the creation of the miracle itself.

The distinction is that what we have heard and memorized becomes imprinted in our hearts, enabling us to repeat it. This illustrates the difference between the miraculous composition and the act of recitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly feel that you are uttering the very speech of God, not something composed by any creature? What would change in your recitation if you internalized this truth tonight?
?

?
If Gabriel himself is merely a manifestation of the Qur'anic speech, how should that affect your reverence when you hear the Qur'an recited by any human voice? Will you listen differently next time?

Miracles

The Subtle Distinction Between Reciting and Composing
Subtle point on recitation vs. composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between our ability to recite the Qur'an and our inability to produce its composition, which proves its miraculous nature.

Core Idea
We can utter the Qur'an because we have heard it, but we cannot create its arrangement.

The author adds a subtle point (al-daqiqa) to further clarify how the Qur'an is miraculous. We can easily recite a poem after hearing it, but we cannot compose it. Similarly, the Qur'an's composition is beyond human power, making recitation possible but creation impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage to understand the subtle point being made.
2
Understand the distinction
The author distinguishes between our ability to utter something we have heard and our inability to compose something with a specific arrangement. This illustrates that the Qur'an's composition is beyond human capacity.
3
Relate to the overall argument
Connect this subtle point to the earlier modalities: the miracle is the composition, not the recitation. This further supports that the Qur'an is divine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل الحق أما السؤال الأول فنقول : القديم يستحيل أن يكون معجزة والمكتسب للعبد كذلك، وللقرآن وجوه من المعجزات ولكل وجه وجوه من التقديرات، فيجوز أن يقال : إن التلاوة من حيث إنها تلاوة معجزة وتقدير الإعجاز فيها من وجهين : أحدهما : أن يخلق الله تعالى هذه الحروف المركبة وتلك الكلمات المنظومة في لسان التالي من غير أن يكون قادرًا عليها ومحركا لسانه بقدرته واستطاعته، فعل الله تعالى، ويظهر إعجازه في نظمه المخصوص، ويجوز أن يخلق الله في نفس النبي كلاما منظوما فيترجم عنه بلسانه، ويكون تحريك اللسان مقدوراً له لكن الكلام المعجز ما اشتمل عليه الضمير ونعت في الصدور، كما قال تعالى: ﴿ولا تحرك به لسانك لتعجل به إن علينا جمعه وقرآنه﴾ أي: في صدرك وقلبك، فإذا قرأناه أي جمعناه فاتبع قرآنه. ويجوز أن يخلق الله ذلك الكلام في قلب الملك أو في لسانه فيلقيه وحيًا إلى قلب النبي ويعبر عنه النبي بلسانه، كما قال تعالى: ﴿إنه لقول رسول كريم﴾ أي تنزيلاً ووحياً، وكما قال: ﴿علمه شديد القوى﴾. ويجوز أن يكون قد خلق الله هذه العبارات المنظومة بعينها في اللوح المحفوظ فيقرأ جبريل عليه السلام منه، ويقرأها على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فيسمع النبي منه كما يسمع الواحد منا ويكون المعجز هو الكلام المنظوم، وجبريل مظهر والنبي مظهر، كما أن واحدًا منا يقرأه ويكون مظهرًا، لكن إظهار جبريل كإظهارنا ليدل على صدق النبي عليه السلام، وهذا كما خلق الله تعالى الناقة في الصخرة ثم أظهرها منها عند دعوة صالح عليه السلام فيكون إظهار المعجزة مقرونا بالدعوى والتحدي، لا إظهار المعجز كخلق المعجز في الدلالة على الصدق. وينبغي أن ننبه هاهنا على هذه الدقيقة وهي أنا إذا روينا شعر الشاعر نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على التلفظ بذلك الشعر، ولا نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على نظم ذلك، بل ربما يكون الراوي عديم الطبع في إنشاء الشعر عن نظم مخصوص، فما المقدور منه وما غير المقدور؟ فنقول : إذا قرأنا شعرا من كتاب أو لسان وحفظناه ارتسم الخيال في القلب والنفس بذلك الشعر وتمكن من عبر بلسانه عنه، فيكون المسموع أو المحفوظ من حيث إنه سمعناه ثم نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على التلفظ به.
The people of truth said: As for the first question, we say: The eternal is impossible to be a miracle, and what is acquired by the servant is likewise. The Qur'an has aspects of miracles, and for each aspect there are aspects of estimations. So it is permissible to say: The recitation, insofar as it is a miraculous recitation, and the estimation of its miraculousness is from two aspects: One of them is that God Most High creates these composite letters and those arranged words in the tongue of the reciter, without him being capable of them or moving his tongue with his power and capacity—an act of God Most High—and He manifests His miracle in its particular arrangement. And it is permissible that God creates in the soul of the prophet arranged speech, and he translates it with his tongue, and the movement of the tongue is within his power, but the miraculous speech is what the conscience contains and what is described in the breasts, as God Most High said: 'Do not move your tongue with it to hasten it; indeed upon Us is its collection and its recitation' [Al-Qiyamah: 16-17], meaning: in your chest and heart. So when We have recited it, i.e., collected it, then follow its recitation [Al-Qiyamah: 18]. And it is permissible that God creates that speech in the heart of the angel or on his tongue, and he casts it as revelation into the heart of the prophet, and the prophet expresses it with his tongue, as God Most High said: 'Indeed it is the word of a noble messenger' [At-Takwir: 19], i.e., a sending down and revelation, and as He said: 'One severe in strength taught him' [An-Najm: 5]. And it is permissible that God has created these very arranged expressions in the Preserved Tablet, so Gabriel, peace be upon him, reads from it, and he reads it to the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, and the Prophet hears from him just as one of us hears, and the miracle is the arranged speech, and Gabriel is a manifestation and the Prophet is a manifestation, just as one of us reads it and is a manifestation. But the manifestation of Gabriel is like our manifestation to indicate the truthfulness of the Prophet, peace be upon him. And this is like when God Most High created the she-camel in the rock and then brought it forth from it at the call of Salih, peace be upon him. So the manifestation of the miracle is coupled with the claim and the challenge, not the manifestation of the miracle like the creation of the miracle in indicating truthfulness. And we should draw attention here to this subtle point: that when we recite the poetry of a poet, we feel within ourselves an ability to utter that poetry, but we do not feel within ourselves an ability to compose it. Indeed, perhaps the narrator is naturally incapable of creating poetry with a particular arrangement. So what part of it is within power and what part is not? We say: When we read poetry from a book or a tongue and memorize it, an image of that poetry is imprinted in the heart and soul, and one is able to express it with his tongue. So what is heard or memorized, insofar as we have heard it, then we feel within ourselves an ability to utter it.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument by the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) responding to a question about the miraculous nature of the Qur'an. The author begins by stating that the eternal (al-qadim) cannot be a miracle, nor can something acquired by the servant (al-muktasab) be a miracle. The Qur'an has multiple aspects of miraculousness, each with various estimations. The discussion then enumerates the possible ways in which the recitation of the Qur'an is considered a miracle, presenting them as two main aspects (but listing four).

The first aspect: God directly creates the letters and words in the tongue of the reciter without the reciter's ability or movement—this is God's act, and the miracle appears in the specific arrangement (al-nazm al-makhsus).

Second aspect: God creates arranged speech in the soul of the prophet, who then translates it with his tongue. The prophet's tongue movement is within his power, but the miraculous speech is what the conscience contains, as indicated by the verse 'Do not move your tongue...' (Q. 75:16-18).

Third aspect: God creates that speech in the heart of the angel (Gabriel) or on his tongue, who then reveals it to the prophet's heart, and the prophet expresses it—supported by verses like 'It is the word of a noble messenger' (Q. 81:19) and 'One severe in strength taught him' (Q. 53:5).

Fourth aspect: God created the exact arranged expressions in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz). Gabriel reads from it and recites to the prophet, who hears it directly. The miracle is the arranged speech itself; both Gabriel and the prophet are manifestations (mazhir) of it. This is analogous to God creating a she-camel in the rock and then bringing it forth as a miracle for Prophet Salih. The manifestation (izhar) of the miracle is coupled with the claim and challenge, not the creation of the miracle itself.

Finally, the author adds a subtle point (al-daqiqa) about poetry: when we recite someone else's poetry, we feel capable of uttering it but not of composing it. The distinction is that what we have heard and memorized becomes imprinted in our hearts, enabling us to repeat it. This illustrates the difference between the miraculous composition and the act of recitation.

Key Terms
نظم
مكتسب
تلاوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that because we can recite the Qur'an, it is within human power to produce it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that recitation is possible only after hearing the composition, which itself is from God. The composition is beyond human ability.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do we feel capable of doing when we hear and memorize a poem?
A) Composing it
B) Reciting it
C) Criticizing it
D) Forgetting it

Key Takeaway
The ability to recite does not imply ability to compose; the Qur'an's composition is the true miracle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an fluently, do you ever feel pride in your own skill? Reflect: does your ability come from your effort or from God's gift of enabling you to hear and memorize? What would change if you truly believed the composition is beyond any human?
?
This lesson says the poet may be unable to compose his own arrangement. You have memorized many verses—have you ever entertained the thought that you could produce something like them? What does this reveal about your heart's reliance on God's word as the only perfect speech?
?
If the Qur'an's miraculous nature lies in its composition, not your recitation, how should your relationship with the text change? Will you now approach each verse with deeper awe, acknowledging that its arrangement is a direct sign from Allah that you could never replicate?

An Analogy: Poetry and Revelation
Analyses human ability to recite versus compose poetry, analogised to impression on sand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy between human inability to compose poetry and the miraculous nature of the Qur'an, and how this demonstrates the Prophet's incapability to produce it.

Core Idea
Reciting is within our power; composing is not—like sand receiving an impression.

The author presents a subtle point: we can recite poetry but cannot compose it. Through the analogy of a board and sand, he argues that the Qur'an's arrival in the Prophet's heart is an impression from God, not his own composition. This establishes the Qur'an as a miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the author's progression from a human experience to a theological conclusion.
Pay attention to the analogy—it is central.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author distinguishes between reciting (uttering) poetry and composing (arranging) it. Why is this distinction important?
The author says the narrator may be devoid of natural talent in composition.
3
Understand the analogy
Analyze the analogy of the board and soft sand. What does the board represent? What does the sand represent? What is within the person's power and what is not?
The act of throwing the board is within power, but the impression is not.
4
Apply the analogy to revelation
See how the author applies the analogy to the Prophet's heart receiving the Qur'an. What is the impression? Who produces it?
The impression in the Prophet's heart is from God, not from himself.
5
Reflect on the miraculous nature
Conclude how this shows that the Qur'an is a miracle: it comes from God, not from the Prophet's own capacity.
This refutes the misconception that the Prophet authored the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينبغي أن ننبه هاهنا على هذه الدقيقة وهي أنا إذا روينا شعر الشاعر نجد من أنفسنا قدرة على التلفظ بذلك الشعر، ولا نحس من أنفسنا قدرة على نظم ذلك، بل ربما يكون الراوي عديم الطبع في إنشاء الشعر على نظم مخصوص، فما المقدور منه وما غير المقدور؟ فنقول : إذا قرأنا شعرا من كتاب أو لسان وحفظناه ارتسم الخيال في القلب والنفس بذلك الشعر وتمكن من عبر بلسانه عنه، فيكون المسموع المحفوظ من حيث إنه سمعناه مقدورًا له لكن النظم المرتب في المحفوظ والمسموع غير مقدور له، وهو كما لو ألقى من لا يعرف الكتابة أصلا لوحا منقورا فيه سورة منظومة على تراب ناعم حتى انتقش بها نقشا مطابقا كان الإلقاء مقدورا له، والنقش غير مقدور له؛ إذ ليس يعرف الكتابة أصلا، فالارتسام في النفس والخيال كالانتقاش في التراب سواء، فيبقى نظم الشعر نقشا بعد نقش زمانًا بعد زمان أبد الدهر، وكذلك ارتسام قلب النبي من إلقاء القرآن إليه وحيا وتنزيلا كارتسام التراب الناعم بالنقش المنقور في اللوح، فيكون المرتسم قلبه، والمعبر عنه لسانه، والرسم غير مقدور له بل تمحض ذلك ابتداء من الله تعالى، فهذا هو طريق وجوده معجزا.
And we should draw attention here to this subtle point: that when we recite a poet's poetry, we find in ourselves ability to utter that poetry, but we do not sense in ourselves ability to compose it, rather perhaps the narrator is devoid of natural talent in poetry regarding a particular composition. So what is within our power and what is not? We say: when we read poetry from a book or a tongue and memorize it, the image of that poetry is impressed in the heart and soul, and one becomes able to express it with his tongue, so what is heard and memorized, in terms of having heard it, is within his power, but the arranged composition in the memorized and heard is not within his power. This is like if someone who does not know writing at all throws a board engraved with a composed surah onto soft sand until it becomes impressed with a matching impression: the throwing is within his power, but the impression is not within his power, because he does not know writing at all. So the impression in the soul and imagination is like the impression in the sand. So the composition of poetry remains an impression after impression, time after time forever. Similarly, the impression in the Prophet's heart from the casting of the Quran to him as revelation and sending down is like the impression of the soft sand with the engraving on the board. So what is impressed in his heart, and what is expressed by his tongue, the impression is not within his power, rather that is purely initiated from God Almighty. This is the way of its existence as a miracle.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage examines the distinction between reciting and composing poetry. It argues that while one can recite memorized poetry, the original composition is not within one's power. The analogy of the board and sand illustrates that the impression (the words) comes from outside. Similarly, the Quran in the Prophet's heart is an impression from God, not produced by the Prophet himself, hence miraculous.

Key Terms
الشعر
النظم
الارتسام
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet produced the Quran himself, as a human composition.
Correction: The passage shows that the Prophet's heart received the Quran as an impression from God, just as sand receives an impression from an engraved board. He had no power to originate it, so it is a miracle from Allah.

Checkpoint
In the analogy, what is within the person's power?
A) The impression on sand
B) The original composition
C) The act of throwing the board
D) The memorization

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an is a miracle because its impression in the Prophet's heart came purely from God, not from the Prophet's own ability to compose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you feel that you are uttering words that you could never have composed on your own? How does this realization increase your awe of its divine origin?
?
The analogy compares the Prophet's heart to soft sand receiving an impression. Is your heart soft and receptive to the Quran's message, or has it become hardened by neglect? What concrete step will you take today to soften your heart?
?
If even the composition of a single line of poetry is beyond the power of a person who cannot compose, how can anyone claim the Quran is from a human? Does this remove any doubt you might have had about its miracle? How will you respond to someone who doubts?

Miracles

The Analogy of the Engraved Board for the Miracle of the Quran
The author gives an analogy explaining how the Quran is a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of the engraved board illustrates that the Quran's composition originates entirely from God, making it a miracle.

Core Idea
The Quran's precise composition is a direct imprint from God, not human effort.

This passage presents a vivid analogy to help us grasp the miraculous nature of the Quran. The author compares the Prophet's reception of revelation to a person who cannot write throwing an engraved board onto soft soil, resulting in a perfect imprint. The analogy clarifies that the Prophet's role is purely receptive, and the Quran's organized form is entirely from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the comparison between the engraved board and the soft soil.
Notice the repeated use of ' نقش ' (imprint) in the Arabic.
2
Identify the key components
The analogy has two sides: the thrower (who cannot write) and the resulting imprint. The thrower's action (throwing) is within his power, but the imprint (patterned text) is not.
3
Apply the analogy to the Prophet
See how the Prophet's heart is like the soft soil, and the Quran's composition is like the engraving. Conclude that the Quran's miraculous existence is established because its precise form is beyond human ability.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحفظه مقدورا له لكن النظم المرتب في المحفوظ والمسموع غير مقدور له، وهو كما لو ألقى من لا يعرف الكتابة أصلا لوحا منقورا فيه سورة منظومة على تراب ناعم حتى انتقش بها نقشا مطابقا كان الإلقاء مقدورا له، والنقش غير مقدور له؛ إذ ليس يعرف الكتابة أصلا، فالارتسام في النفس والخيال كالانتقاش في التراب سواء، فيبقى نظم الشعر نقشا بعد نقش زمانا بعد زمان أبد الدهر، وكذلك ارتسام قلب النبي من إلقاء القرآن إليه وحيا وتنزيلا كانتقاش التراب الناعم بالنقش المنقور في اللوح، فيكون المرتسم قلبه، والمعبر عنه لسانه، والرسم غير مقدور له بل تمحض ذلك ابتداء من الله تعالى، فهذا هو طريق وجوده معجزا.
And its preservation is within his power, but the organized composition in what is preserved and heard is not within his power, and it is like when someone who does not know writing at all throws a board engraved with a surah arranged onto soft soil until it impresses upon it a matching imprint: the throwing was within his power, but the imprinting is not within his power, because he does not know writing at all. So the impression in the soul and imagination is like the imprint in soil — the same. Thus the composition of poetry remains imprint after imprint, time after time, forever. Likewise, the impression on the heart of the Prophet from the casting of the Quran to him as revelation and sending down is like the imprint of the soft soil by the engraving engraved in the board. So what is imprinted is his heart, and what is expressed by his tongue, and the imprint is not within his power; rather that originated entirely from God Almighty. This is the way of its existence as a miracle.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage provides an analogy to demonstrate the miraculous nature of the Quran. The author compares the Prophet's reception of the Quran to a person who cannot write throwing an engraved board onto soft soil, resulting in a perfect imprint that the thrower did not create. Just as the thrower is not the cause of the imprint, the Prophet is not the cause of the Quran's composition; it is entirely from God. The analogy emphasizes that the Quran's precise and organized form is beyond human ability, thus proving it a miracle.

Key Terms
معجز
ارتسام
نقش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet composed the Quran from his own knowledge or creativity.
Correction: The analogy shows he had no ability to write or compose such poetry; the Quran's form is a direct imprint from God, not a product of human effort.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of the engraved board and soft soil illustrate about the Quran?
A) The Prophet's active composition of the Quran
B) That the Quran's precise arrangement originates from God alone
C) The preservation of the Quran in the Prophet's memory
D) The Prophet's ability to control revelation

Key Takeaway
The Quran's miraculous nature lies in its precise composition, which is directly from God, not from human effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly feel that you are speaking words that are directly imprinted from God, or are they just words you have memorized through your own effort? How does this analogy challenge the sense of ownership you might have over your recitation? What would change if you approached each verse as if it were being engraved on your heart right now?
?
Is your heart soft soil, ready to receive the imprint of God's revelation, or has it become hardened by your own opinions, desires, and intellectual pride? This lesson says the Prophet's heart was purely receptive—are you willing to set aside your own compositions and let the Quran reshape you without resistance?
?
You may believe the Quran is from God intellectually, but this passage forces you to confront a deeper question: Are you living as if the Quran's composition is a direct imprint from God, or do you treat it as something you can master and control? What specific area of your life needs to become 'soft soil' for the Quran to imprint itself anew?

The Qur'an's Inimitability in Eloquence
Sanusi argues the Quran's eloquence is incomparable and defines its inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's claim that the Quran surpassed all Arab speech in eloquence and that its inimitability lies in its rhetorical perfection.

Core Idea
The Quran's eloquence is not merely better, but entirely outside the category of human speech.

This lesson introduces the central claim of the Qur'an's miraculous nature: it surpassed all forms of Arab speech in eloquence, succinctness, and composition. The author explains that the Arabs' inability to produce anything like it was absolute, not a loss of ability. This sets the foundation for understanding the miracle of the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the Quran surpassing Arab speech
Read the first part where the author states that the Quran surpassed dialogues, correspondences, sermons, and poems in eloquence (fasaaha), succinctness (jazaala), and composition (nazm).
2
Understand the nature of the Arabs' incapacity
Notice that the author specifies it was absolute incapacity from beginning to end, not a temporary inability. This is crucial to the argument.
3
Identify the definition of i'jaz
The author concludes that the Quran is miraculous (mu'jiz) with respect to rhetoric (al-balaghah), which is the sum of these three qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المعلوم أن القرآن فاق كلام العرب بمحاورتها ومراسلاتها وخطبها وأشعارها فصاحة وجزالة ونظما؛ بحيث عجزت عن معارضتها عجز من لم يقدر عليها أولا وآخرا، لا عجز من قدر في الأول وعجز في الآخر، وإلا لكانوا يعارضونه بما تمهد عندهم من الكلام الأول، فالقرآن معجز من حيث البلاغة التي هي عبارة عن مجموع المعاني الثلاثة، والعرب قد أحست من أنفسها أن القرآن خارج عن جنس كلامهم جملة، وكذلك كل من كان له أدنى معرفة بالعربية يعرف إعجازه، إلا أن البلغاء يعرفون وجوه الإعجاز فيه على قدر مراتبهم في البلاغة، ومن كان أفصح وأبلغ كانت معرفته أشد وأوضح، كما أن من كان أسحر في زمن موسى كان علمه ومعرفته بإعجاز العصا أوفر، ومن كان أحذف في الطب في زمن عيسى كانت معرفته بإعجاز إحياء الموتى وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص أكثر، ومن كان أعلم في علم الطبيعة في زمن الخليل كان نفسه بإعجاز السلامة عن النار أشد وأصدق. والاختلاف في وجوه الإعجاز لا يوهن وجه الإعجاز، ولهذا لو اجتمع عند العالم بوجوه الإعجاز العلم بوجوه الحكم والمعاني الشريفة فيه كانت معرفته أكثر، ولو ضم إلى ذلك العلم بوجوه تمهيد السياسات العامة والعبادات الخاصة والأمر بمكارم الأخلاق والنهي عن ذميمات الأفعال، والحث على معالي الشيم والهمم كانت معرفته بالإعجاز في غاية الانتظام ونهاية الإبرام، ومن ذا الذي يصل بفكره الضعيف وعقله الممير بمحاديات الوهم والخيال إلى الحكم المحققة في تركيب الحروف وترتيب الكلمات التي هي كالمواد والصور، ومطابقتهما لعالم الخلق حتى ينطبق عالم الأمر على عالم الخلق، وذلك العلم الأخص بالأنبياء عليهم السلام، وهنالك تضع الأفهام وتكل الأوهام، وتعود العقول البشرية عند الأسرار الإلهية هباء وتستحيل الحواس الإنسانية عند خواص الحكم الربانية عفاء.
And it is known that the Qur'an surpassed the speech of the Arabs in their dialogues, correspondences, sermons, and poems in eloquence, succinctness, and composition, such that they were incapable of opposing it – the incapacity of one who was never able to do so from first to last, not the incapacity of one who was able at first and then incapable later, otherwise they would have opposed it with what they had prepared of earlier speech. So the Qur'an is miraculous (muʿjiz) with respect to rhetoric (al-balāghah), which is the sum of the three meanings. The Arabs themselves felt that the Qur'an was entirely outside the genus of their speech, and likewise everyone who has the slightest knowledge of Arabic knows its inimitability (iʿjāz), except that the eloquent (al-bulaghāʾ) know the aspects of inimitability in it according to their ranks in eloquence. The more eloquent and rhetorically accomplished one is, the stronger and clearer his knowledge, just as one who was more skilled in sorcery in the time of Moses had greater knowledge and recognition of the miracle of the staff, and one who was more skilled in medicine in the time of Jesus had more knowledge of the miracle of reviving the dead and healing the blind and leper, and one who was more knowledgeable in the science of nature in the time of Abraham had stronger and truer certainty of the miracle of safety from fire. And difference in the aspects of inimitability does not weaken the aspect of inimitability. For this reason, if one who knows the aspects of inimitability also has knowledge of the aspects of wisdom and noble meanings in it, his knowledge is greater; and if he adds to that knowledge of the aspects of laying down general policies and specific acts of worship, commanding noble character and forbidding blameworthy deeds, and urging lofty virtues and aspirations, then his knowledge of inimitability reaches the utmost order and perfection. Who can reach with his weak intellect and his mind? – which is perplexed by the pulls of imagination and fancy – the verified judgments concerning the composition of letters and arrangement of words, which are like matter and form, and their correspondence to the world of creation, such that the world of command corresponds to the world of creation? That knowledge is most specific to the prophets (peace be upon them). There, understandings fall short and imaginations tire, human intellects become as nothing before divine secrets, and human senses turn to dust before the properties of lordly wisdom.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for the Qur'an's inimitability (iʿjāz) based on its rhetorical perfection (al-balāghah). The author begins by stating a known fact: the Qur'an surpassed all forms of Arab speech – everyday conversation, correspondence, sermons, poetry – in eloquence, succinctness, and composition. The Arabs' inability to produce anything like it is described as absolute: they were never able, not that they lost ability after having it. If they had once been able, they would have produced something similar from their earlier stock. Thus the Qur'an is miraculous in its rhetoric, which is the combination of the three qualities. The Arabs themselves sensed that it was entirely outside the category of their speech, and anyone with minimal Arabic recognizes its miraculous nature. However, the full appreciation of its miraculous aspects depends on one's degree of rhetorical skill: the more eloquent person perceives the miracle more deeply. The author draws analogies: a skilled sorcerer in Moses' day better appreciated the staff's miracle; a skilled physician better understood Jesus' healings; a natural philosopher better grasped Abraham's safety from fire. Differences in recognizing the aspects of iʿjāz do not undermine the miracle itself; rather, deeper knowledge of the Qur'an's wisdom, moral teachings, and social principles only enhances one's recognition. The passage then points to the ultimate depth: the correspondence between the composition of letters/words and the structure of creation, a knowledge reserved for prophets. Human intellects fail before such divine secrets.

Key Terms
إعجاز
بلاغة
فصاحة
جزالة
نظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Arabs were initially able to produce something like the Quran but later lost that ability.
Correction: The author explicitly states it was 'عجز من لم يقدر عليها أولا وآخرا' – the incapacity of one who never had the ability from the beginning to the end. So they never possessed the ability at any point.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what type of incapacity did the Arabs have regarding the Quran?
A) They were able at first but later became unable
B) They were never able to produce anything like it at any time
C) They did not try because they admired it
D) Only the most eloquent Arabs failed

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability lies in its unparalleled rhetorical perfection, which the Arabs were never able to match, proving it is beyond human capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard the claim that the Qur'an is incomparable. But when you read it, do you feel its eloquence piercing your heart, or has repetition made its words slide past you without impact? What will you do today to listen to a single verse as if hearing it for the first time?
?
The Arabs were humbled by their own inability. Where in your life have you proudly relied on your own skill, talent, or intelligence, only to be confronted by something that proves you powerless? Will you admit that primal inability and turn to the One who sent that challenge?
?
If you truly believed that the Qur'an surpasses every beautiful word you have ever heard—every poem, speech, or song that moves you—how would that change what you choose to fill your ears and heart with? What will you cut out, and what will you replace it with?

Recognizing the Miracle: Levels of Understanding
Sanusi discusses varying levels of recognizing the Quran's inimitability and uses analogies to other prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that recognition of the Quran's inimitability varies with one's eloquence and that differences in perception do not undermine the miracle itself.

Core Idea
The more eloquent you are, the deeper you perceive the Quran's miracle – but all can recognize it.

This lesson explores how different people recognize the Quran's inimitability. The author explains that even those with minimal Arabic know it is miraculous, but the eloquent understand its aspects more deeply. He draws analogies with the miracles of Moses, Jesus, and Abraham to show that expertise in the relevant field enhances appreciation. The passage also emphasizes that differing opinions on the aspects of inimitability do not weaken the miracle, and that ultimate knowledge of its deeper layers is reserved for prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the section on recognition of i'jaz
Read from 'والعرب قد أحست' to the end of the passage. Notice the claim that even minimal Arabic knowledge suffices to know the Quran's inimitability.
2
Understand the analogies to other prophets
The author compares the recognition of i'jaz to how a skilled sorcerer in Moses' time, a skilled physician in Jesus' time, and a natural philosopher in Abraham's time would better appreciate their respective miracles.
These analogies illustrate that expertise in the relevant domain deepens recognition.
3
Grasp the principle: differences do not weaken the miracle
The author states that differences in understanding the aspects of i'jaz do not undermine the miracle itself. This counters a potential objection.
4
Reflect on the deeper knowledge of composition
The passage concludes with the idea that the deepest understanding of the Quran's composition and its correspondence to creation is reserved for prophets, beyond human intellect.
This part is profound and humbling.

The Quran's Inimitability and Its Depths
Al-Shahrastani explains the Quran's multifaceted inimitability and the varying degrees of its perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran's inimitability is universally acknowledged, but its full comprehension varies according to the observer's knowledge and that disagreement on its aspects does not undermine the miracle itself.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle is undeniable; its depths are known proportionally to the observer's insight.

Al-Shahrastani opens by stating that even minimal Arabic knowledge suffices to recognize the Quran's inimitability, yet the eloquent perceive its aspects according to their eloquence. He draws parallels with other prophets' miracles to show that expertise enhances recognition. This passage establishes that the miracle is not diminished by differing interpretations, and that the highest understanding belongs to the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Notice the analogy with Moses, Jesus, and Abraham.
2
Identify the key analogy
Pay attention to how the author compares recognition of i'jaz to recognition of previous miracles by experts in relevant fields.
3
Understand the progression
Trace how the author builds from basic recognition to deeper levels, culminating in knowledge exclusive to prophets.
The rhetorical question highlights human limitation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the Qur'anic verse and its meaning for the inexhaustibility of Allah's words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان له أدنى معرفة بالعربية يعرف إعجازه، إلا أن البلغاء يعرفون وجوه الإعجاز فيه على قدر مراتبهم في البلاغة، ومن كان أفصح وأبلغ كانت معرفته أشد وأوضح، كما أن من كان أسحر في زمن موسى كان علمه ومعرفته بإعجاز العصا أوفر، ومن كان أحذف في الطب في زمن عيسى كانت معرفته بإعجاز إحياء الموتى وإبراء الأكمه والأبرص أكثر، ومن كان أعلم في علم الطبيعة في زمن الخليل كان نفسه بإعجاز السلامة عن النار أشد وأصدق. والاختلاف في وجوه الإعجاز لا يوهن وجه الإعجاز، ولهذا لو اجتمع عند العالم بوجوه الإعجاز العلم بوجوه الحكم والمعاني الشريفة فيه كانت معرفته أكثر، ولو ضم إلى ذلك العلم بوجوه تمهيد السياسات العامة والعبادات الخاصة والأمر بمكارم الأخلاق والنهي عن ذميمات الأفعال، والحث على معالي الشيم والهمم كانت معرفته بالإعجاز في غاية الانتظام ونهاية الإبرام، ومن ذا الذي يصل بفكره الضعيف وعقله المبهوت بمجاذبات الوهم والخيال إلى الحكم المحققة في تركيب الحروف وترتيب الكلمات التي هي كالمواد والصور، ومطابقتهما لعالم الخلق حتى ينطبق عالم الأمر على عالم الخلق، وذلك العلم الأخص بالأنبياء عليهم السلام، وهنالك تضع الأفهام وتكل الأوهام، وتعود العقول البشرية عند الأسرار الإلهية هباء وتستحيل الحواس الإنسانية عند خواص الحكم الربانية عفاء. ولو أن ما في الأرض من شجرة أقلام والبحر يمده من بعده سبعة أبحر ما نفدت كلمات الله [لقمان: ٢٧].
Everyone who has even the least knowledge of Arabic knows its inimitability, except that the eloquent know the aspects of inimitability in it according to their degrees in eloquence. And whoever was more eloquent and more persuasive, his knowledge is stronger and clearer, just as whoever was more magical in the time of Moses had greater knowledge and recognition of the miracle of the staff, and whoever was more skilled in medicine in the time of Jesus had more recognition of the miracle of reviving the dead and curing the blind and leprous, and whoever was more knowledgeable in natural science in the time of al-Khalil (Abraham) had a stronger and more truthful conviction of the miracle of safety from fire. And disagreement regarding the aspects of inimitability does not weaken the aspect of inimitability. For this reason, if one who knows the aspects of inimitability also possesses knowledge of the aspects of wisdom and noble meanings in it, his knowledge is greater. And if to that is added knowledge of the aspects of establishing general policies, specific acts of worship, commanding noble morals, forbidding blameworthy deeds, and encouraging high traits and ambitions, then his knowledge of inimitability reaches the utmost order and final perfection. And who can reach, with his weak thought and his mind bewildered by the tugs of illusion and imagination, to the verified judgments concerning the composition of letters and arrangement of words, which are like matter and form, and their correspondence to the world of creation, such that the world of command corresponds to the world of creation? That knowledge is specific to the prophets, peace be upon them. There, intellects weaken and imaginations become exhausted, and human intellects turn to dust before divine secrets, and human senses turn to decay before the properties of divine wisdom. And if all the trees on earth were pens and the sea, replenished by seven seas after it, were ink, the words of Allah would not be exhausted [Luqman 27].
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This is an argumentative passage that defines the nature of the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz). The author begins by establishing that even minimal knowledge of Arabic suffices to recognize the Quran's miracle, but those with higher eloquence perceive its aspects more deeply. He draws analogies with other miracles: Moses' staff, Jesus' healing, and Abraham's safety from fire, showing that expertise in the relevant field enhances recognition of the miracle. He then argues that differing opinions about the aspects of i'jaz do not invalidate the miracle itself. Adding knowledge of wisdom, political systems, worship, ethics, and high character further increases one's perception of its miracle. A rhetorical question emphasizes the impossibility of fully comprehending the divine composition of letters and words, which correspond to the worlds of creation and command—a knowledge exclusive to prophets. The passage concludes with the Quranic verse (Luqman 27) underscoring the inexhaustibility of Allah's words, reinforcing the infinite depth of the Quran's i'jaz.

Key Terms
إعجاز
بلاغة
وجوه الإعجاز
الحكم المحققة
عالم الأمر
عالم الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement about the aspects of inimitability weakens the miracle itself.
Correction: The author explicitly states that differing opinions do not weaken the aspect of inimitability; the core miracle remains intact.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for why different people perceive the Quran's inimitability differently?
A) The Quran was revealed in stages.
B) People have varying degrees of eloquence and knowledge.
C) The miracle is only apparent to non-believers.
D) The Arabic language is inherently ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is recognized by all, but its full depth is known only to the prophets; disagreement on its aspects does not detract from its miraculous nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that even minimal Arabic knowledge suffices to recognize the Quran's inimitability. Yet, do you truly feel that awe when you recite or hear the Quran? What does your lack of heartfelt response reveal about your engagement with the Book? Commit today to recite one verse with full presence and ask Allah to open your heart to its miracle.
?
The passage teaches that true depth of understanding requires combining knowledge of wisdom, ethics, worship, and governance. Which of these areas have you neglected in your study of the Quran? How will you expand your focus to experience the i'jaz more profoundly? Write down one practical step for this week.
?
The deepest secrets of the Quran's composition are known only to prophets, and human intellects become exhausted before them. Does this humble you, or do you secretly pride yourself on your understanding? In what area of your faith do you rely on your own reasoning rather than submitting to divine wisdom? Repent and ask the Prophet ﷺ for intercession to truly appreciate the Quran.

The Eloquence of the First Revealed Verse
Al-Shahrastani demonstrates the profound eloquence and wisdom in the first revealed verse (Surah Al-Alaq 1-5).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how even the first short verse of the Quran demonstrates profound eloquence and wisdom, challenging the view that inimitability is limited to long passages.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability is evident even in its shortest verse.

Al-Shahrastani begins by critiquing those who think the Quran's miraculous nature is only in long surahs. He then invites the reader to examine the very first verse revealed, showing that it contains layers of eloquence, wisdom, and theological depth. This lesson explores his analysis and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the author's critical tone at the beginning.
2
Understand the criticism
Identify who the author is criticizing and why. This sets the stage for his demonstration.
3
Explore the aspects of eloquence
The author lists several features of the verse: the generality of creation, specification to humans, the use of 'Lord', the progression from clot, and the connection to Surah Ar-Rahman. Read the commentary to grasp each.
4
Reflect on the demonstration
Consider how even a short verse can carry such depth. This shows that the Quran's inimitability is not confined to length or specific verses.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage criticizes those who limit the Quran's inimitability to a long sura or specific verses, arguing that even the very first revealed verse (Surah Al-Alaq 1-5) contains profound eloquence and wisdom. The author invites the reader to examine how it expresses the concept of creatorship universally and then specifically for humans, whose creation mirrors the world. He points out the use of the name 'Lord' (al-Rabb) in the context of nurturing, the generalization of creation before specification, and the progression from a clot to the reception of revelation, linking it to Surah Ar-Rahman's stages of teaching the Quran and eloquence. The analysis highlights the coupling of 'Recite' with 'Your Lord is the Most Generous' and the teaching by the pen. The text ends abruptly, but the intended point is that even a single verse demonstrates the Quran's unmatched rhetorical and theological depth.

Levels of Quranic Inimitability (I'jaz)
Explains the varying levels of I'jaz based on audience knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz) is not uniform but varies according to the audience's knowledge and insight.

Core Idea
The Quran's miraculousness is measured by the listener's depth of understanding.

This lesson explores a key concept in Islamic theology: that the Quran's miraculous nature is not fixed but relative to the audience. The great theologian al-Shahrastani explains how the same Quran is miraculous at different levels—from the entire Book to a single verse—depending on the reader's rhetorical and theological awareness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense rhetorical description at the beginning.
2
Identify the four levels of i'jaz
The passage lists four levels at which the Quran is miraculous. Find each one and note what type of person each level applies to.
The levels are: whole Quran, ten surahs, one surah, one verse.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself why the Quran's i'jaz is not the same for everyone. The commentary will help you connect this to the audience's knowledge and verification.
This connects to the learning objective.

Most eloquent in wording and meaning than 'Read in the name of your Lord' then He generalized learning then specified man, just as He generalised creation then specified man. And how eloquence gathered in words with [؟] faces of meanings, and how it was accompanied by this magnificence despite the fewness of its letters and lightness of its words without deficiency in signification, and how it was arranged with the noblest composition in terms of wording, and clearest order in terms of meaning, and how rhetoric appeared in it with smoothness of words and solidity of meanings until it was as if it gathered the knowledge of the first and the last from establishing generality and particularity, and the precedence of command over creation, and attributing guidance and creation to Him (Blessed is God, Lord of the worlds). So it became clear from these indications that the Quran in its entirety is miraculous, and that is with respect to one who does not know the subtleties of rhetoric and the realities of meanings; and with ten surahs from it it is miraculous with respect to some people; and with one surah it is miraculous with respect to some people; and with one verse it is miraculous with respect to some people; and the measures of inimitability are estimated according to the degree of knowledge and verification.
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the varying levels of the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz). The author begins by illustrating the Quran's eloquence through the example of the command 'Read' (Iqra) and how it combines generality and specificity. He then describes how the Quran's eloquence gathers multiple layers of meaning with few letters, achieving rhetorical perfection. The key conclusion is that the Quran is miraculous at different levels: the entire Quran is a miracle for those ignorant of rhetoric; ten surahs suffice for some; one surah for others; and even a single verse is miraculous for others, varying according to the listener's knowledge and insight. This refutes the notion that the Quran's i'jaz is uniform or that it depends solely on length.

Correction: The Quran's i'jaz varies: the entire Quran is a miracle for the ignorant, while a single verse suffices for the knowledgeable. The level of inimitability is proportionate to the audience's understanding.
